Defining Milk (Possession) as a State or Relation
Al-Jurjani defines milk (possession) and discusses its nature as a state versus relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of milk (possession) as a state that moves with its enclosing object and the argument against reducing it to a mere relation.

Core Idea
Possession is a state that travels with what it encloses.

In this passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif, al-Jurjani examines the category of milk (possession). He defines it as a state that occurs to a thing due to what surrounds it and moves with it. He then discusses whether it is better understood as a relation or a state, and concludes with a linguistic note connecting the term to richness.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of milk
Start by reading the Arabic definition: milk is a state that arises from something surrounding the object and moves with the object's movement. Note the key condition: movement with the object.
This distinguishes milk from mere location (ayn).
2
Examine the critique of the relation view
Al-Jurjani references al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya, which defines milk as a relation. He argues this is a loose usage; it is actually a relative state, because the relations of being enclosed and enclosing are symmetric.
3
Note the linguistic note on 'jidda'
The term jidda (richness) is given as an alternative name for milk, which fits the concept of possession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع الملك ويسمى الجدة أيضا وهو هيئة تعرض للشي بسبب ما يحيط به وينتقل بانتقاله وبهذا القيد الأخير أعني انتقال . قوله : (ويسمى الجدة ) بمعنى الغناء. قوله : ( وهو هيئة تعرض إلخ) في المباحث المشرقية أنَّه عبارة عن نسبة الجسم إلى حاصر له أو لبعضه وينتقل بانتقاله فجعله نفس النسبة والحق أنه تسامح والمراد أنه أمر نسبي حاصل للجسم بسبب أمر حاصر لأن نسبة المحصورية ونسبة الحاصرية مستويتان فجعل إحداهما مقولة . قوله : ( ويسمى الجدة الجدة في اللغة الغناء فيناسب الملك .
Fourth: al-milk (possession) – also called al-jidda – is a state that occurs to a thing due to what surrounds it and moves with its movement. And by this last condition, i.e. movement. His saying: (also called al-jidda) meaning richness. His saying: (and it is a state that occurs etc.) In al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya it is: an expression for the relation of the body to something that encloses it or part of it and moves with its movement. So he made it the relation itself. The truth is that this is a loose usage, and what is meant is a relative matter that occurs to the body due to an enclosing thing, because the relation of being enclosed and the relation of enclosing are symmetric, and making one of them a category is... His saying: (and it is called al-jidda) al-jidda in language is richness, so it suits possession.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage defines the accidental category milk (possession), also called jidda. It is defined as a state that occurs to a thing due to what surrounds it and moves with its movement. The condition of moving with it distinguishes milk from other relations like location. The commentary notes that in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya, milk is defined as the relation itself between the body and the enclosing thing. But the author (al-Jurjani) considers this a simplification; it is actually a relative state caused by the enclosing thing. The two relations (being enclosed and enclosing) are symmetric, so one is not more fundamental.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possession (milk) is the same as location (ayn) because both involve a surrounding thing.
Correction: The key difference is that in milk, the surrounding thing moves with the object, whereas in location, it does not.

Checkpoint

A) It involves a surrounding thing
B) The surrounding thing moves with the object
C) It is a state

Key Takeaway
Milk (possession) is a state that moves with its enclosing object, distinguishing it from static location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your possessions are merely a state that moves with the object, and not a fixed reality, how does that challenge your sense of permanent ownership? Will you hold more lightly to what you have?
?
The definition of milk involves being surrounded by something that moves with you. What surrounds you that defines your identity—your wealth, job, or relationships? Are you aware that these are mere states, not your core self?
?
When you recognize that possession is a temporary, moving state, does it increase your desire to share or your anxiety about losing? What would change in your daily life if you truly internalized this?

Definition of al-Wad' and the Argument for Two Relations
Defines and proves that position depends on two relations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the accident of position (al-wad') must be defined as a state caused by both internal and external relations, and why ignoring the external relation leads to the absurd conclusion that standing and inversion are identical.

Core Idea
Position is a state caused by two relations; without the external, standing and inversion would be the same.

In this passage, the author establishes the essential definition of position (al-wad') as a simple state resulting from two relations: the relation of a body's parts to each other and their relation to external matters. He uses a striking thought experiment: if only the internal relation were considered, then a standing person turned upside down (inversion) would have the same position — an absurd conclusion that proves the need for the external relation. The objection that they share a genus and differ by a differentia is also refuted.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text from the beginning to 'فالحق إذن اعتبار النسبتين في ماهية الوضع.' Identify key terms like الوضع, الهيئة, النسبة, القيام, الانتكاس.
The passage includes a definition, a proof, an objection, and a refutation.
2
Understand the definition
The author defines position as a state (hay'a) caused by two relations: the relation of parts to each other and the relation of parts to external matters. Note the examples of standing and lying down.
This is the standard definition in kalam.
3
Follow the reductio argument
The author argues: if only the internal relation were considered, then turning a standing person upside down would not change the internal arrangement, so the state caused by that relation alone would be identical. Thus standing and inversion would be the same position, which is false.
This shows the necessity of the external relation.
4
Examine the objection and refutation
The objector says: the two share the genus of position and differ by a differentia from external relations. The refutation: genus and differentia are united in existence; you cannot have a portion of the genus that changes its differentia.
This reinforces that both relations are essential to the essence of position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالقرب والبعد والمحاذاة وغيرها ( و ) بسبب نسبة أجزائه ) إلى الأمور الخارجة ) عن ذلك الشيء كوقوع بعضها نحو السماء مثلا وبعضها نحو الأرض وإذا جعل الوضع هيئة معلولة لنسبتين معا ( فالقيام والاستلقاء وضعان ( متغايران ) لاختلاف نسبة الأجزاء ) فيهما ( إلى الخارج ولو لم يعتبر في ماهية الوضع نسبة الأجزاء إلى الأمور الخارجة بل اكتفى فيها بالنسبة فيما بين الأجزاء وحدها لزم أن يكون القيام بعينه الانتكاس لأن القائم إذا قلب بحيث لا تتغير النسبة فيما بين أجزائه كانت الهيئة المعلولة لهذه النسبة وحدها باقية بشخصها فيكون وضع الانتكاس وضع القيام بعينه لا يقال اللازم مما ذكرتم اشتراكهما في معنى الوضع الذي هو جنسهما فجاز أن يفترقا بالفصل الحاصل من النسبة الخارجية لأنَّا نقول الجنس والفصل يتحدان وجوداً وجعلاً فكيف يتصوران حصة من الجنس قارنت فصلاً ثم فارقته إلى فصل آخر فالحق إذن اعتبار النسبتين في ماهية الوضع. قوله : ( وإذ جعل الوضع إلخ) اتفقوا على أن الوضع هيئة بسيطة معلولة للنسبتين وليس مركباً منهما إذ النسبة فيما بين الأجزاء أو فيما بينها وبين الأمور الخارجة ليس إلا القرب والبعد والمحاذاة والمجاورة والتماس وليس القيام والقعود نفس تلك النسب ولا مركباً من الهيئتين الحاصلتين من تينك النسبتين إذ لا دليل على وجودها في القيام مثلا فضلا عن تركبه منهما فهو هيئة وحدانية معلولة لهما فتدبر فإنه مما زل فيه الأقدام واعلم أنه عرف الإمام الوضع في المباحث المشرقية : بأنه هيئة تحصل للجسم بسبب نسبة بعض أجزائه إلى بعض نسبة تخالف الأجزاء لأجلها بالقياس إلى الجهات كالموازاة والانحراف ولا تخالف بين التعريفين وإن كان ظاهر هذا التعريف مشعراً بأنه معلول لنسبة الأجزاء فيما بينها لأنه قيد فيه النسبة بكونها موجبة لتخالفها بالقياس إلى الجهات وذلك لا يحصل إلا بعد اعتبار القسمة إلى الأمور الخارجة أيضاً إلا أنَّه في التعريف المشهور جعل معلولاً لمجموع النسبتين وفيما ذكره الإمام معلولاً للنسبة المقيدة. قوله : ( بحيث لا تتغير النسبة فيما بين أجزائه وأما كون الأجزاء التحتانية في القيام فوق الأجزاء الفوقانية فيه في الانتكاس فراجع إلى اعتبار نسبة الأجزاء إلى الأمور الخارجية لأن فوقيتها عبارة عن قربها من المحيط.
By proximity, distance, alignment, and other [relations], and because of the relation of its parts to external matters, like some of its parts being toward the sky for example and some toward the earth. And if position (al-wad') is made a state (hay'a) caused by two relations together, then standing (al-qiyām) and lying down (al-istiqlā') are two different positions, due to the difference of the relation of the parts to the exterior. And if one did not consider in the essence of position the relation of the parts to external matters, but sufficed in it with the relation among the parts alone, then standing would necessarily be the same as inversion (al-intikās), because if the standing person is turned over in such a way that the relation among his parts does not change, then the state caused by this relation alone remains identical in its particularity, so the position of inversion would be exactly the position of standing. It cannot be said: the necessary consequence from what you mentioned is that they share in the meaning of position which is their genus, so it is possible that they differ by the differentia obtained from the external relation; because we say: the genus and the differentia are united in existence and constitution, so how can one conceive a portion of the genus that accompanied a differentia then left it for another differentia? So the truth is to consider both relations in the essence of position. His saying (may God be pleased with him): 'And if position is made [a state caused by two relations etc.]' — They agreed that position is a simple state (hay'a basīṭa) caused by the two relations and not composed from them, since the relation among the parts or between them and external matters is nothing but proximity, distance, alignment, adjacency, and contact. And standing and sitting are not the same as those relations nor composed from the two states resulting from those two relations, since there is no evidence for their existence in standing, for example, let alone its composition from them. So it is a unitary state (hay'a waḥdāniyya) caused by them. So reflect, for it is one of those [matters] in which feet slip. And know that the Imam [Fakhr al-Din al-Razi] defined position in al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya as: a state that occurs for a body due to the relation of some of its parts to others, a relation that makes the parts differ in relation to directions, like parallelism and deviation. There is no discrepancy between the two definitions, although the apparent meaning of this definition suggests that it is caused by the relation of the parts among themselves, because he restricted the relation to being productive of their difference with respect to directions, and that does not occur except after considering the division into external matters as well. However, in the well-known definition, it is made caused by the sum of the two relations, and in what the Imam mentioned, it is caused by the restricted relation. His saying: 'such that the relation among his parts does not change' — and as for the fact that the lower parts in standing become above the upper parts in inversion, that refers back to considering the relation of the parts to external matters, because their being above is an expression of their proximity to the [celestial] sphere.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage defines the accident of position (al-wad') and proves that it depends on two relations: the relation of a body's parts to each other and the relation of those parts to external matters. The author first states that position is a state (hay'a) caused by both relations together, giving the example of standing and lying down — they differ because the parts relate differently to the outside (e.g., in standing, the head is up; in lying, it is horizontal). Then he argues: if only the internal relation among the parts were considered, then turning a standing person upside down (inversion) would not change the internal arrangement, so the state caused solely by that internal relation would remain identical; thus standing and inversion would be the same position, which is absurd. The author anticipates an objection: that standing and inversion share the genus of position but differ by a differentia from external relations. He refutes it by noting that genus and differentia are united in existence and cannot be separated; a particular instance of a genus cannot be characterized by one differentia and then switch to another. Therefore, both the internal and external relations must be included in the essence of position. The subsequent commentary clarifies that position is a simple state (not composite) caused by two relations, not composed from them. It also compares this definition with that of Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, who defined position as a state resulting from a relation among parts that makes them differ with respect to directions — which implicitly includes external relations as well. Finally, a gloss explains that the condition 'such that the relation among his parts does not change' in the inversion example actually depends on external relations (e.g., 'above' means proximity to the celestial sphere).

Key Terms

الهيئة
النسبة
القيام
الاستلقاء
الانتكاس
الجنس
الفصل

Checkpoint
What would happen if position (al-wad') were defined solely by the internal relation among a body's parts, ignoring external relations?
A) Standing and inversion would become identical positions.
B) Position would become a composite of multiple states.
C) The definition would match that of Imam al-Razi.
D) Position would become a type of relation.

Key Takeaway
Position (al-wad') is essentially defined by both internal and external relations; ignoring either leads to absurd consequences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold strongly. Is it defined only by its internal consistency, or have you considered how it relates to external realities (e.g., your actions, your community, the world)? What might you discover if you examined that external relation more closely?
?
The passage argues that genus and differentia are united in existence—you cannot have a 'portion of genus' that switches differentiae. How does this challenge the way you sometimes compartmentalize your own identity (e.g., as a student, a friend, a believer)? Is your 'essence' a collection of separable traits, or is it a unified whole?
?
If standing and inversion were identical just because internal arrangement stayed the same, the world would be absurd. In your life, are there situations where ignoring external context leads you to treat very different states as the same? When have you mistaken a superficial similarity for real identity?

Clarifications and Imam al-Razi's Definition of al-Wad'
Clarifies the unitary nature of al-wad' and compares definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that al-wad' is a simple state caused by two relations (not composite), and how Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi's definition aligns with the well-known one.

Core Idea
Al-wad' is a simple unitary state caused by two relations, not composed from them.

After establishing the need for two relations, the author clarifies that position (al-wad') itself is a simple state (hay'a basīṭa), not a composite of two states. He then compares this definition with that of Imam al-Razi, who defined position as a state due to a relation among parts that makes them differ with respect to directions. The author shows that the two definitions are equivalent, as al-Razi's implicitly includes external relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read from 'قوله : ( وإذ جعل الوضع إلخ)' to the end. Note the key claim that position is a simple state caused by two relations.
The passage also includes a warning: 'it is one of those in which feet slip' — indicating conceptual difficulty.
2
Understand the simplicity of al-wad'
The author argues that position is not composed of two states from the two relations; rather, it is a unitary state caused by them. The relations themselves are just proximity, distance, etc., which are not the position itself.
There is no evidence for two separate states combining.
3
Compare with Imam al-Razi's definition
Imam al-Razi defined position as a state due to a relation among parts that makes them differ with respect to directions. This implicitly includes external relations because 'directions' refer to external orientation.
The author says there is no discrepancy between the two definitions.
4
Examine the gloss on the inversion example
A gloss explains that the condition 'such that the relation among his parts does not change' in the inversion example actually presupposes external relations, because 'above' is defined relative to the celestial sphere.
This reinforces the unity of the two relations.

Source Text

بالقرب والبعد والمحاذاة وغيرها ( و ) بسبب نسبة أجزائه ) إلى الأمور الخارجة ) عن ذلك الشيء كوقوع بعضها نحو السماء مثلا وبعضها نحو الأرض وإذا جعل الوضع هيئة معلولة لنسبتين معا ( فالقيام والاستلقاء وضعان ( متغايران ) لاختلاف نسبة الأجزاء ) فيهما ( إلى الخارج ولو لم يعتبر في ماهية الوضع نسبة الأجزاء إلى الأمور الخارجة بل اكتفى فيها بالنسبة فيما بين الأجزاء وحدها لزم أن يكون القيام بعينه الانتكاس لأن القائم إذا قلب بحيث لا تتغير النسبة فيما بين أجزائه كانت الهيئة المعلولة لهذه النسبة وحدها باقية بشخصها فيكون وضع الانتكاس وضع القيام بعينه لا يقال اللازم مما ذكرتم اشتراكهما في معنى الوضع الذي هو جنسهما فجاز أن يفترقا بالفصل الحاصل من النسبة الخارجية لأنَّا نقول الجنس والفصل يتحدان وجوداً وجعلاً فكيف يتصوران حصة من الجنس قارنت فصلاً ثم فارقته إلى فصل آخر فالحق إذن اعتبار النسبتين في ماهية الوضع. قوله : ( وإذ جعل الوضع إلخ) اتفقوا على أن الوضع هيئة بسيطة معلولة للنسبتين وليس مركباً منهما إذ النسبة فيما بين الأجزاء أو فيما بينها وبين الأمور الخارجة ليس إلا القرب والبعد والمحاذاة والمجاورة والتماس وليس القيام والقعود نفس تلك النسب ولا مركباً من الهيئتين الحاصلتين من تينك النسبتين إذ لا دليل على وجودها في القيام مثلا فضلا عن تركبه منهما فهو هيئة وحدانية معلولة لهما فتدبر فإنه مما زل فيه الأقدام واعلم أنه عرف الإمام الوضع في المباحث المشرقية : بأنه هيئة تحصل للجسم بسبب نسبة بعض أجزائه إلى بعض نسبة تخالف الأجزاء لأجلها بالقياس إلى الجهات كالموازاة والانحراف ولا تخالف بين التعريفين وإن كان ظاهر هذا التعريف مشعراً بأنه معلول لنسبة الأجزاء فيما بينها لأنه قيد فيه النسبة بكونها موجبة لتخالفها بالقياس إلى الجهات وذلك لا يحصل إلا بعد اعتبار القسمة إلى الأمور الخارجة أيضاً إلا أنَّه في التعريف المشهور جعل معلولاً لمجموع النسبتين وفيما ذكره الإمام معلولاً للنسبة المقيدة. قوله : ( بحيث لا تتغير النسبة فيما بين أجزائه وأما كون الأجزاء التحتانية في القيام فوق الأجزاء الفوقانية فيه في الانتكاس فراجع إلى اعتبار نسبة الأجزاء إلى الأمور الخارجية لأن فوقيتها عبارة عن قربها من المحيط.
By proximity, distance, alignment, and other [relations], and because of the relation of its parts to external matters, like some of its parts being toward the sky for example and some toward the earth. And if position (al-wad') is made a state (hay'a) caused by two relations together, then standing (al-qiyām) and lying down (al-istiqlā') are two different positions, due to the difference of the relation of the parts to the exterior. And if one did not consider in the essence of position the relation of the parts to external matters, but sufficed in it with the relation among the parts alone, then standing would necessarily be the same as inversion (al-intikās), because if the standing person is turned over in such a way that the relation among his parts does not change, then the state caused by this relation alone remains identical in its particularity, so the position of inversion would be exactly the position of standing. It cannot be said: the necessary consequence from what you mentioned is that they share in the meaning of position which is their genus, so it is possible that they differ by the differentia obtained from the external relation; because we say: the genus and the differentia are united in existence and constitution, so how can one conceive a portion of the genus that accompanied a differentia then left it for another differentia? So the truth is to consider both relations in the essence of position. His saying (may God be pleased with him): 'And if position is made [a state caused by two relations etc.]' — They agreed that position is a simple state (hay'a basīṭa) caused by the two relations and not composed from them, since the relation among the parts or between them and external matters is nothing but proximity, distance, alignment, adjacency, and contact. And standing and sitting are not the same as those relations nor composed from the two states resulting from those two relations, since there is no evidence for their existence in standing, for example, let alone its composition from them. So it is a unitary state (hay'a waḥdāniyya) caused by them. So reflect, for it is one of those [matters] in which feet slip. And know that the Imam [Fakhr al-Din al-Razi] defined position in al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya as: a state that occurs for a body due to the relation of some of its parts to others, a relation that makes the parts differ in relation to directions, like parallelism and deviation. There is no discrepancy between the two definitions, although the apparent meaning of this definition suggests that it is caused by the relation of the parts among themselves, because he restricted the relation to being productive of their difference with respect to directions, and that does not occur except after considering the division into external matters as well. However, in the well-known definition, it is made caused by the sum of the two relations, and in what the Imam mentioned, it is caused by the restricted relation. His saying: 'such that the relation among his parts does not change' — and as for the fact that the lower parts in standing become above the upper parts in inversion, that refers back to considering the relation of the parts to external matters, because their being above is an expression of their proximity to the [celestial] sphere.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage defines the accident of position (al-wad') and proves that it depends on two relations: the relation of a body's parts to each other and the relation of those parts to external matters. The author first states that position is a state (hay'a) caused by both relations together, giving the example of standing and lying down — they differ because the parts relate differently to the outside (e.g., in standing, the head is up; in lying, it is horizontal). Then he argues: if only the internal relation among the parts were considered, then turning a standing person upside down (inversion) would not change the internal arrangement, so the state caused solely by that internal relation would remain identical; thus standing and inversion would be the same position, which is absurd. The author anticipates an objection: that standing and inversion share the genus of position but differ by a differentia from external relations. He refutes it by noting that genus and differentia are united in existence and cannot be separated; a particular instance of a genus cannot be characterized by one differentia and then switch to another. Therefore, both the internal and external relations must be included in the essence of position. The subsequent commentary clarifies that position is a simple state (not composite) caused by two relations, not composed from them. It also compares this definition with that of Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, who defined position as a state resulting from a relation among parts that makes them differ with respect to directions — which implicitly includes external relations as well. Finally, a gloss explains that the condition 'such that the relation among his parts does not change' in the inversion example actually depends on external relations (e.g., 'above' means proximity to the celestial sphere).

الهيئة البسيطة
النسبة
المباحث المشرقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That position (al-wad') is a composite of two states resulting from the two relations.
Correction: Position is a simple, unitary state caused by the two relations, not composed from them.

Checkpoint

B) It is equivalent but expressed differently.
C) It omits the internal relation.
D) It adds a third relation.

Key Takeaway
Al-wad' is a simple state caused by two relations, and different formulations of its definition are ultimately equivalent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The text says position is a simple state caused by two relations, not composed from them. Yet we often mistake the causes for the thing itself. In your relationship with Allah, what external and internal factors do you mistake for the state of your iman? How can you focus on the simple reality of your faith rather than its contributing relations?
?
Reflecting on the warning 'feet slip' in this subtle matter, when have you slipped by overcomplicating a simple truth about your deen? What would it take to accept the simplicity of that truth without adding or subtracting?
?
Al-Razi's definition implicitly includes external factors through the concept of directions. Think of an area in your life where you have ignored external context when assessing something internal. How can you train yourself to see both relations together, as the source passage demands?

Definition of al-Milk and Its Distinction from Place
Defines al-milk as a state from an encompassing thing that moves with it and distinguishes it from place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of possession (al-milk) as a state arising from an encompassing thing that moves with the object, and how it differs from place (al-makān).

Core Idea
Possession is a state that travels with the thing; place does not.

This passage defines the category of al-milk (possession), also called al-judda (wealth), and carefully distinguishes it from place (al-makān). The key distinction is that the encompassing thing in possession moves with the object, whereas place remains stationary. The text also clarifies the nature of this state and addresses a potential counterexample.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the full definition of al-milk.
Notice the emphasis on movement as the distinguishing feature.
2
Identify the distinguishing feature
Find the sentence that explicitly distinguishes possession from place. Understand why the movement of the encompassing thing is crucial.
The text says: 'By this last qualification... possession is distinguished from place.'
3
Classify types of encompassment
The passage gives two pairs of categories for the encompassing thing: natural vs. artificial, and whole vs. partial. Note the examples for each.
Examples: skin (natural), garment (artificial, whole), ring (partial).
4
Examine the commentary on al-judda and the nature of possession
Read the commentary explaining the synonym al-judda and the debate over whether possession is a relation or a state. Understand the preferred view.
The correct view is that possession is a relative state (amr nisbī) caused by an encompassing thing.
5
Address the waterskin objection
Consider the potential counterexample of an inflated waterskin, where the inner surface (place) seems to move with the air. The commentary clarifies that place does not move completely.
This will be discussed further in the investigation of place.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الرابع الملك ) ويسمى الجدة أيضا ( وهو هيئة تعرض للشي بسبب ما يحيط به وينتقل بانتقاله وبهذا القيد الأخير أعني انتقال المحيط بانتقال المحاط ) يمتاز ( الملك ) عن المكان ) أي الأين المتعلق به فإنه وإن كان هيئة عارضة للشيء بسبب المكان المحيط به إلا أن المكان لا ينتقل بانتقال المتمكن ( سواء كان ( ذلك المحيط أمراً ( طبيعياً ) خلقياً ( كالإهاب ) للهرة مثلاً ( أو لا يكون طبيعياً (و) سواء كان ( محيطاً بالكل كالثوب) الشامل لجميع البدن ( أو ) محيطاً بالبعض كالخاتم والعمامة والخف والقميص وغيرها. قوله : ( ويسمى الجدة ) بمعنى الغناء. قوله : ( وهو هيئة تعرض إلخ) في المباحث المشرقية أنَّه عبارة عن نسبة الجسم إلى حاصر له أو لبعضه وينتقل بانتقاله فجعله نفس النسبة والحق أنه تسامح والمراد أنه أمر نسبي حاصل للجسم بسبب أمر حاصر لأن نسبة المحصورية ونسبة الحاصرية مستويتان فجعل إحداهما مقولة. قوله : ( ويسمى الجدة الجدة في اللغة الغناء فيناسب الملك . قوله : ( لا ينتقل بانتقال المتمكن قيل : المراد أنّه لا ينتقل بانتقاله كلياً كيلا ينتقض بالزق المنفوخ فإنَّ سطحه الباطن مكان الهواء الداخل فيه وينتقل بانتقاله كما إذا سكن تحت الماء ثم خلي وسيأتي الكلام على مثله في بحث المكان .
The fourth [category]: possession (al-milk), also called wealth (al-judda). It is a state that occurs for a thing due to what encompasses it and moves with its movement. By this last qualification, namely the encompassing thing moving with the movement of the encompassed, possession is distinguished from place (al-makān), i.e., the 'where' (al-ayn) related to it. For although [place] is a state occurring for the thing due to the place encompassing it, nevertheless place does not move with the movement of the thing located [in it]. Whether that encompassing thing is natural, like the skin for a cat, for example, or not natural; and whether it encompasses the whole, like a garment covering the entire body, or encompasses a part, like a ring, turban, shoe, shirt, and others. His saying: 'and it is also called al-judda' — meaning wealth (al-ghinā'). His saying: 'it is a state that occurs etc.' — In al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya: It is [defined as] a relation of the body to something encompassing it or part of it, and moving with its movement, so he made it the relation itself. The truth is that this is a looseness; the intended meaning is that it is a relative matter (amr nisbī) occurring for the body due to an encompassing thing, because the relation of being encompassed and the relation of encompassing are correlative, so making one of them a category [is sufficient]. His saying: 'and it is called al-judda' — al-judda in language is wealth (al-ghinā'), so it suits possession. His saying: 'it does not move with the movement of the thing located' — It is said: the intended [sense] is that it does not move with its movement completely, so as not to be contradicted by the inflated waterskin, for its inner surface is the place of the air inside it, and it moves with its movement, as when it settles under water then is released. The discussion of such cases will come later in the investigation of place.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage defines the category of possession (al-milk) and distinguishes it from place (al-makān), which is also known as the 'where' (al-ayn). The definition: possession is a state that arises for a thing due to something that encompasses it and moves with the movement of the thing (the encompassed). The crucial distinguishing feature is that the encompassing thing moves with the moved object. In contrast, place (e.g., the location where a thing sits) does not move with the thing located in it. The encompassing medium can be natural (like the skin of a cat) or artificial (like a garment), and it can encompass the whole thing (like a full-body garment) or only a part (like a ring). The commentary then explains that the term 'al-judda' (wealth) is synonymous with 'al-milk' in this context. It also notes a definition from al-Mabāḥith al-Mashriqiyya that treats possession as a relation between the body and its container, but the author clarifies that this is a loose expression; the correct view is that possession is a relative state resulting from an encompassing something, since the relation of being encompassed and the relation of encompassing are correlative and cannot be separated as distinct categories. Finally, a gloss addresses a potential objection: how can possession be said to move with the object when place (e.g., the inner surface of a waterskin) seems to move with the air it contains? The answer is that place does not move completely with the object—only partially, as in the waterskin example, which will be further discussed in the section on place.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possession is nothing but a relation between the body and its container.
Correction: The correct view is that possession is a state caused by an encompassing thing, not merely a relation, because the relation of being encompassed and encompassing are correlative and cannot be a single category.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does this truth confront your daily attachments? Will you deliberately detach from one of them today?
?

?

Definition of al-Idafah and Distinction from Absolute Relation
Defines al-idafah as a twofold relation and distinguishes it from mere relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-idāfa as a repeated proportion and how it differs from absolute proportion.

Core Idea
Relation is a proportion that points to another proportion.

This lesson introduces the fifth category of accident: relation (al-idāfa). The author defines it as a 'repeated proportion' — a proportion that is itself understood in relation to another proportion. Through the examples of fatherhood-sonship and place-location, we learn to distinguish genuine relations from mere proportions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall meaning.
2
Understand 'repeated proportion' through fatherhood and sonship
Notice how fatherhood is a proportion that only makes sense in relation to sonship, and vice versa. This is the core idea of al-idāfa.
3
Examine the place-location example
The author contrasts two cases: when place relates to the essence of the thing located (resulting in al-ayn, not idāfa) versus when place relates to the thing located as possessing a place (resulting in idāfa). Understand why the latter is a repeated proportion.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Consider why the existence of something in a place is a mere proportion, not a relation. Then try to apply the same reasoning to other examples (e.g., knowledge and knower).
This step solidifies the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس الإضافة وهي النسبة المتكررة أي نسبة تعقل بالقياس إلى نسبة ) أخرى معقولة أيضا بالقياس إلى الأولى ( كالأبوة فإنها نسبة تعقل بالقياس إلى البنوة وإنها ) أي البنوة أيضاً ( نسبة ) تعقل بالقياس إلى الأبوة فالإضافة أخص من مطلق النسبة ( فإذا نسبنا المكان إلى ذات المتمكن حصل للمتمكن باعتبار الحصول فيه ( هيئة هي الأين وإذا نسبناه إلى ( المتمكن باعتبار ) كونه ذا مكان كان الحاصل ) مضافاً ) لأن لفظ المكان يتضمن نسبة معقولة بالقياس إلى نسبة أخرى هي كون الشيء ذا مكان أي متمكناً فيه فالمكاتية والمتمكنية من مقولة الإضافة وحصول الشيء في المكان نسبة تعقل بين ذاتي الشيء والمكان لا نسبة معقولة بالقياس إلى نسبة أخرى فليس من المقولة ( وبهذا) الذي صورناه لك ( يمكنك الفرق بين النسبة التي ليست من المضاف ( و ) بين (المضاف فاعقله وتحققه في سائر النسب فإنه مما قد طول فيه ) الكلام ) وحاصله ما قلنا : قوله : ( فالإضافة إلخ ) خص الإضافة بالذكر مع أنَّ جميع المقولات كذلك لخفاء الحكم فيها . قوله : ( إلى ذات المتمكن أي مع قطع النظر عن وصف التمكن.
The fifth [category]: relation (al-idāfa), which is the repeated proportion (al-nisba al-mutakarrira), i.e., a proportion that is conceived in relation to another proportion also conceived in relation to the first, like fatherhood, for it is a proportion conceived in relation to sonship, and it (i.e., sonship also) is a proportion conceived in relation to fatherhood. So relation is more specific than absolute proportion. For if we relate a place (al-makān) to the essence of the thing located (al-mutamakkin), there results for the thing located, by virtue of being possessed in it, a state that is the 'where' (al-ayn). And if we relate it to the thing located by virtue of its being possessed of a place, then what results is a relative [term] (muḍāf), because the word 'place' implies a proportion conceived in relation to another proportion, which is the being of the thing possessed of a place, i.e., being located in it. So the state of being placed (al-makāniyya) and the state of being located (al-mutamakkin) are from the category of relation. And the existence of something in a place is a proportion conceived between the two essences of the thing and the place, not a proportion conceived in relation to another proportion; so it is not from the category [of relation]. And by this that we have depicted for you, you can distinguish between the proportion that is not of the relative (al-muḍāf) and the relative. So comprehend it and verify it in other proportions, for it is something over which lengthy speech has been given. The upshot is what we have said. His saying: 'relation (al-idāfa) etc.' — He specified relation [from among the categories] although all categories are like that, because the judgment regarding it is obscure. His saying: 'to the essence of the thing located' — i.e., disregarding the description of being located.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage defines the fifth category: relation (al-idāfa). It is called the 'repeated proportion' because it is a proportion that is itself understood in relation to another proportion, which is also understood in relation to the first. Example: fatherhood is a proportion conceived in relation to sonship, and sonship is a proportion conceived in relation to fatherhood. Thus, relation is more specific than absolute proportion (mutlaq al-nisba). The author then illustrates with place: if we relate a place to the essence of a thing located in it, the result is the 'where' (al-ayn), which is a state of the thing. But if we relate the place to the thing located by virtue of its being possessed of a place, the result is a relative term (muḍāf) because the word 'place' implies a proportion to another proportion (i.e., the thing's being located). Consequently, the state of being a place (al-makāniyya) and the state of being located (al-mutamakkiyya) belong to the category of relation. In contrast, the mere existence of a thing in a place is a proportion between the two essences (thing and place) and is not a proportion conceived in relation to another proportion; therefore it is not from the category of relation. The author concludes by emphasizing that this distinction enables one to differentiate between a proportion that is not relative and a relative proportion, and urges the reader to understand and verify it in other cases. The commentary notes that al-idāfa is singled out for discussion despite all categories being similar because the judgment concerning relation is subtle. Another gloss clarifies that 'to the essence of the thing located' means disregarding the attribute of being located (i.e., considering only the essence).

Key Terms
الإضافة (al-idāfa)
النسبة المتكررة (al-nisba al-mutakarrira)
النسبة المطلقة (al-nisba al-muṭlaqa)
الأبوة (al-ubuwwa)
البنوة (al-bunuwwa)
المضاف (al-muḍāf)
المكان (al-makān)
المتمكن (al-mutamakkin)
الأين (al-ayn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any relation between two things, such as a book being on a table, is a case of al-idāfa (the category of relation).
Correction: Only those proportions that are themselves conceived in relation to another proportion count as al-idāfa. For example, a book being on a table is a mere proportion between two essences, not a repeated proportion, so it is not al-idāfa.

Checkpoint
Why is fatherhood (al-ubuwwa) considered a relation (al-idāfa) according to the passage?
A) Because it involves two individuals.
B) Because it is a proportion conceived in relation to another proportion (sonship).
C) Because it is a state of the father.
D) Because it is a category of accident.

Key Takeaway
Al-idāfa is a proportion that is conceived in relation to another proportion; not every correlation between two things qualifies as a category of relation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that a true relation (al-idāfa) requires mutual reference—you cannot be a father without someone being your son. In your own life, are there relationships you have that are, in reality, one-sided? What would it take for them to become a genuine mutual relation? And if you are a servant ('abd) of Allah, does your heart feel that mutual reference: that He is your Lord and you are His servant, or is it just a label?
?
The author distinguishes a mere proportion (like a book on a table) from a relation (like father-son). Which of your daily ties—your job, your friendships, your possessions—are merely functional proportions, and which are true relations that define who you are? Will you reflect on just one today and ask yourself: does this relation connect me back to the One who gave it meaning?
?
The passage says that the judgment regarding al-idāfa is 'obscure' and needs careful attention. Think about your own understanding of how you relate to your Creator. Is your relationship with Him a 'repeated proportion'—something that defines you and that you define by Him—or have you reduced it to a mere 'existence in a place' (a passive belief)? What step will you take today to make your servitude to Allah a true mutual relation, not just a fact?

Defining Action as a Non-Stable State of Affecting
Defines the category of action as a transient state of affecting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the category of 'an yaf‘al' refers to the transient state of affecting, not the agent or the principle of the action.

Core Idea
Action is a passing state, not the doer.

This passage from the commentary on al-Mawaqif clarifies the sixth category: 'that he does' (an yaf‘al). It defines action as the transient affect, using the example of a heater heating. The key distinction is between the action itself and the enduring principle (the heater). Let's examine how the text establishes this.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the example of the heater (المسخن) and the description of the state as 'non-stable' (غير قارة).
2
Identify the definition of 'an yaf‘al'
The passage defines 'an yaf‘al' as التأثير (affecting). It then specifies that this affecting is a non-stable state (حالة غير قارة) that belongs to the category of 'that he does'. Underline this point.
The definition is not the agent, but the affect itself.
3
Distinguish action from agent
The text explicitly says 'an yaf‘al is other than the principle of heat (the heater)'. This is the core distinction: the heater remains after the heating stops, but the heating itself is transient.
This distinction is crucial in kalam for discussing divine attributes and actions.
4
Understand the commentary on pronoun reference
The commentator clarifies that the pronoun in 'an yaf‘al' refers to the heating (التسخين) derived from the example, not the heater. Even though heating and the act of heating are the same in essence, the judgment changes depending on the aspect considered.
This shows the nuance of kalam terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس أن يفعل وهو التأثير كالمسخن مادام يسخن) فإن له ما دام يسخن حالة غير قارة هي التأثير التسخيني الذي هو من مقولة أن يفعل ( أن يفعل ) إذن غير ما هو مبدأ للسخونة ) أي المسخن ( لأنه يبقى بعد . قوله : ( يعني أن يفعل إلخ ) المطابق لسياق الكلام أن يفسر الضمير بالتسخين المستفاد من التمثيل إلا أنَّه لما كان الحكم بكون التسخين مغايراً للمسخن بديهياً لا يليق أن يذكر في فضلا أن عن يفرع على كون مقولة الفعل متجدداً فتنزه بأن يفعل وهو وإن كان نفس التسخين إلا أن الحكم يختلف باختلاف العنوان .
The sixth [category]: 'that he does' (an yaf‘al), i.e., affecting (al-ta'thīr), like the heater as long as it heats. For it, as long as it heats, has a non-stable state (ḥāla ghayr qārrā), which is the heating affect (al-ta'thīr al-taskhīnī) that belongs to the category of 'that he does'. So 'that he does' is other than the principle of heat (mabda' al-sukhūna), i.e., the heater, because it remains afterwards. His saying: 'means that he does etc.' — What corresponds to the context of the speech is that the pronoun is interpreted as [referring to] the heating (al-taskhīn) understood from the example. However, since the judgment that heating is different from the heater is obvious, it is not fitting to mention it, let alone to derive from it that the category of action is transient; so he says 'that he does', and even if it is the very heating, judgment varies according to the different aspects.
Source pages p. 20

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth category: 'that he does' (an yaf‘al), which is the category of action. It is defined as affecting (al-ta'thīr), exemplified by a heater as long as it is heating. The heater has, while it is heating, a non-stable state (ḥāla ghayr qārrā) which is the heating affect itself. This affect belongs to the category of 'that he does'. Importantly, 'that he does' is not the same as the principle of heat (the heater itself), because the principle (the heater) remains after the affecting ceases. The commentator clarifies that the pronoun in 'an yaf‘al' refers to the heating action, not the heater. He notes that the fact that heating is different from the heater is obvious, so it might not need mention. The reason for mentioning it is to show that the category of action is transient (mutajaddid). Even though the action is the same as the heating in essence, the judgment differs depending on whether one considers it as an action (transient) or as a source (remaining).

Key Terms
أن يفعل (an yaf‘al)
التأثير (al-ta'thīr)
المسخن (al-musakhkhin)
التسخين (al-taskhīn)
مبدأ السخونة (mabda' al-sukhūna)
حالة غير قارة (ḥāla ghayr qārrā)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'an yaf‘al' refers to the agent or the stable principle of the action.
Correction: The passage clearly states that 'an yaf‘al' is the non-stable state of affecting, distinct from the agent (the heater) which remains after the action.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'an yaf‘al' (that he does) refer to?
A) The heater itself.
B) The non-stable state of affecting (the heating).
C) The principle of heat.
D) The stable state of being hot.

Key Takeaway
The category of 'an yaf‘al' denotes the transient affect of an action, not the enduring agent or principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

This lesson teaches that action is a non-stable state. Right now, identify one act you did today that you are still thinking about. Can you release it as a passing affect and still find your worth in your enduring servitude to Allah?
?
The heater remains after the heating stops. In your life, what are the 'stable principles' that define you beyond your actions? This lesson forces you to separate your doings from your being. Write down three actions you have done this week that you are proud of. Now imagine them gone. Who are you without them? If your answer is 'nothing,' then you have mistaken your transient acts for your essence. Ask Allah to show you the difference and to make your identity rooted in Him alone.

The Two Aims of the Classification of Accidents Are Not Certain
The commentator refutes the certainty of the two aims in the classification of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator argues that the philosophers' classification of accidents into nine highest genera is not certain.

Core Idea
The nine categories of accidents are not proven to be the only or highest genera.

The philosophers claimed that accidents are divided into nine highest genera. But the commentator points out that this claim has not been proven with certainty. In this lesson, we will examine his reasoning: he shows that both the claim that these nine are highest genera and the claim that there are no others are open to possibility and thus not certain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the commentator's use of 'it is possible' and 'not established'.
This passage is a critical commentary; the author is showing uncertainty.
2
Identify the two aims
The passage mentions two aims: first, that the nine are highest genera; second, that there is no other category. Note how each is challenged.
3
Understand the first refutation
The commentator argues that each category might not be a genuine genus; what is under it could be different in reality, making the commonality accidental.
This would make it a common accident, not a genus.
4
Understand the second refutation
Even if they are genera, they might not be highest: they could be simple, middle, or lowest genera.
The passage gives two possibilities for this.
5
Reflect on the enumeration
The second aim (no other category) is also not established because another category could exist beyond the nine.
Thus the classification is not certain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يشتمل على مقامين أحدهما أنَّ هذه التسعة أجناس عالية، والثاني أنَّه ليس للأعراض عال سواها وليس شيء من هذين المقامين بيقيني وذلك ( أنَّه لم يثبت كون كل واحد من التسعة جنساً لما تحته لجواز أن يكون ما تحته أموراً مختلفة بالحقيقة وهو عارض لها ) فيكون حينئذ عَرَضاً عاماً لا جنساً ( ولا كونها ) أي ولم يثبت أيضاً كون هذه التسعة على تقدير جنسيتها ( أجناساً عالية لجواز أن يكون ما تحتها أنواعاً حقيقية (فيكون كل واحد منها حينئذ (جنساً مفرداً لا عالياً (أو) أن ( يكون اثنان منها أو أكثر داخلاً تحت جنس) آخر ( فيكون ) ذلك الداخل تحت الجنس الآخر ( جنساً متوسطاً ) إن كان ما تحته أجناساً ( أو ) جنساً (سافلاً) إن كان ما تحته أنواعاً حقيقية فظهر أنه لم يثبت المقام الأول بل نقول لم يتصد أحد منهم لإثباته أصلا ( ولا الحصر) أي ولم يثبت أيضاً الحصر الذي هو المقام الثاني (لجواز مقولة أخرى) أي جنس عال للأعراض مغاير للتسعة المذكورة
It includes two aims: one that these nine are highest genera, and the second that there is no higher genus for accidents besides them. And nothing from these two aims is certain. That is because it has not been established that each one of the nine is a genus for what is under it, because it is possible that what is under it are things differing in reality and it (the alleged genus) is accidental to them, so it would then be a common accident not a genus. Nor has it been established that these nine, supposing they are genera, are highest genera, because it is possible that what is under them are real species, so each one of them would then be a simple genus (not highest), or that two or more of them fall under another genus, so that which falls under another genus would be a middle genus if what is under it are genera, or a lowest genus if what is under it are real species. So it appears that the first aim is not established—rather we say no one among them has attempted to prove it at all. Nor is the enumeration established, i.e., also the second aim is not established, because another category is possible, i.e., a highest genus for accidents other than the mentioned nine.
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on the classification of accidents into nine categories by the philosophers. The commentator states that the text contains two aims: first, that these nine are the highest genera, second, that there is no other genus above them for accidents. However, the commentator argues that neither of these aims is certain. For the first aim, it is not proven that each of the nine is truly a genus, because what falls under it might be things that are really different, and the commonality could be merely accidental, making it a common accident rather than a genus. For the second aim, even if we suppose they are genera, they might not be highest: what is under them could be real species, making them simple genera that are not highest; or two or more of them might fall under a higher genus, making them middle or lowest genera. Thus the first aim is unestablished, and no one has even attempted to prove it. As for the enumeration, the second aim is also unestablished because there could be another category beyond the nine. This sets up the need for a proper proof, which Ibn Sina provides later.

Key Terms
مقامان (two aims)
أجناس عالية (highest genera)
عرض عام (common accident)
جنس مفرد (simple genus)
حصر (enumeration)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosopher's classification of accidents into nine highest genera is definitive and certain.
Correction: The commentator shows that it is not certain because there are plausible possibilities that contradict both the claim that the nine are highest and the claim that there are no others.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator say that the first aim (that the nine are highest genera) is not certain?
A) Because some of the nine are not genera at all.
B) Because what is under each may be different in reality, making it a common accident rather than a genus.
C) Because there is no evidence for the existence of these nine.
D) Because the philosophers never discussed this aim.

Key Takeaway
The classification of accidents into nine highest genera is not certain because plausible alternatives undermine both claims.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The possibility of another highest genus for accidents reminds us that human knowledge is limited. How does this affect your trust in your own reasoning? Will you depend more on divine revelation for certainty in matters of creed and practice?

Objection: Relation to Stationary Quantity Not Limited to Encircling
Objection and commentary on the enumeration of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the relation to a stationary quantity is not limited to encircling, and how the commentary defends the enumeration of accidents.

Core Idea
Not all relations to stationary quantities are reduced to 'ain and malik.

This lesson presents a critical objection to Ibn Sina's classification of accidents, specifically regarding the relation to stationary quantity (al-kamm al-qar). The objector argues that encircling (al-iḥāṭa) is not the only mode of relation, thereby challenging the enumeration. The commentary provides a series of clarifications to uphold the classification. We will examine the objection and the commentary's responses step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning to understand the objection: 'we do not concede that the relation to a stationary quantity is only by encircling, so that it is restricted to 'ain'.
Note that the objection targets the exclusivity of encircling.
2
Examine the commentary's clarifications
The commentary addresses several points: the interpretation of 'parts of its subject', the repetition of relation in idafa, and the action/passion relation to quality.
These clarifications show that the enumeration is not undermined.
3
Understand the response about shape
The commentary counters that even if relation by encircling is accepted, it is not restricted to the two types, because shape (al-shakl) is also an encircling relation not covered.
This strengthens the enumeration by showing additional examples.
4
Address the counterexample of 'now'
The commentary resolves a potential issue with mata (when) regarding the 'now', stating that the relation to the boundary of time is mediated by time itself.
This shows the comprehensiveness of the original classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( والاعتراض) على ما ذكر في هذا الحصر ( أنا لا نسلم أن النسبة إلى الكم القار تكون بالإحاطة فقط ) حتى تنحصر في الأين
قوله : (أي الأجزاء موضوعها إلخ ) هذا هو المطابق لما في الشفاء وإن كان عبارة المتن يحتمل نسبة الأجزاء فيما بينهما وإلى الأمور الخارجة . قوله : ( لأن النسبة حينئذ (متكررة فيه أن اعتبار النسبة إلى نسبة أخرى لا يقتضي اعتبار النسبة الثانية بالقياس إلى الأولى حتى تتكرر النسبة ولا يرد هذا على عبارة الشيخ فإنه قال : وأما الذي يوجب نسبة فإما أن يوجب نسبة تجعل الماهية معقولة بالقياس إلى المنسوب إليه ويكون هناك انعكاس متشابه في معنى النسبة وهذا هو الإضافة .
قوله : ( بأن يكون منه غيره أي يكون غير الكيف حاصلاً من الكيف كالحرارة والبرودة المؤثرتين فيما يجاور الماء والنار . قوله : (وهو) أي الكون المذكور.
قوله: (ويكر هو من غيره أي يكون الكيف حاصلاً من غيره كالبرودة والحرارة الحاصلتين فيما يجاور محلهما .
قوله : (لا يقبل النسبة لذاته فإنَّ الجواهر لانفسها لا يستحق أن يجعل لها أو إليها نسبة بل إنما يستحق لأمور ولأحوال فيها كذا في الشفاء. قوله : ( يكون بالإحاطة فقط) ولو سلم فالنسبة بالإحاطة غير منحصرة فيهما لأنَّ الشكل هيئة إحاطة كم مقدار بمقدار وليس شيئا منهما .
قوله : ( أي لأجزاء (موضوعها عبارة المتن تحتمل نسبة الأجزاء إلى الأمور الخارجية أيضاً لكن مذهب أبي علي اعتبار النسبة الواحدة في الوضع فلذا فسر عبارة المتن بما ذكر . قوله : ( وإن كان غير قار فهو متى فإن قلت : قد سبق أن النسبة في متى قد يكون إلى طرف الزمان أعني الآن كما في الحروف الآنية وقد خرجت عن تقسيم الشيخ فما توجيهه قلت : النسبة إلى طرف الزمان نسبة إلى زمان بواسطة.
And the objection to what was mentioned in this enumeration is: we do not concede that the relation to a stationary quantity is only by encircling, so that it is restricted to 'ain. [Commentary:] His saying: 'i.e., the parts of its subject, etc.' This corresponds to what is in al-Shifa, although the text's expression may indicate a relation of parts to each other and to external things. His saying: 'because the relation then is repeated' contains the point that considering a relation to another relation does not require considering the second relation in relation to the first such that the relation is repeated, and this does not apply to the Shaykh's statement, for he said: that which entails a relation either entails a relation that makes the essence understood in relation to that to which it is related, and there is a symmetrical reflection in the meaning of the relation, and this is al-idafa. His saying: 'by being from other than it' means that something other than the quality arises from the quality, like heat and cold affecting what is adjacent to water and fire. His saying: 'and it is' meaning the mentioned state. His saying: 'and it is from other than it' means that the quality arises from other than it, like cold and heat arising in what is adjacent to their place. His saying: 'does not accept relation by itself' because substances by themselves do not deserve to have a relation made to them or from them; rather they deserve it for things and states in them, as in al-Shifa. His saying: 'is only by encircling' and even if so, the relation by encircling is not restricted to those two because shape is a form of encircling of a quantity by a quantity, and it is not one of them. His saying: 'i.e., for the parts of its subject' the text's expression also may indicate a relation of parts to external things, but the doctrine of Abu Ali is the consideration of a single relation in position, so he interpreted the text accordingly. His saying: 'and if it is non-stationary, then it is mata' then if you say: it has preceded that the relation in mata may be to the boundary of time, i.e., the 'now', as in instantaneous letters, and it is outside the Shaykh's division, so what is its justification? I say: the relation to the boundary of time is a relation to time by mediation.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to Ibn Sina's enumeration of accidents. The objector denies that the relation to a stationary quantity (al-kamm al-qar) is only by encircling (al-iḥāṭa), which would restrict it to 'ain (where) and malik (possession). The commentary then provides a series of clarifications. First, it explains that the phrase 'i.e., for the parts of its subject' matches al-Shifa, but the text could also be interpreted differently. Second, it addresses the repetition of relation in idafa, explaining that it does not entail infinite regress. Third, it clarifies that 'by being from other than it' refers to action (an yaf'ala) and passion (an yanfa'ila) as relations to quality. Fourth, it explains that substances do not accept relation by themselves. Fifth, it responds to the objection by noting that even if relation by encircling is accepted, it is not restricted to the two mentioned types, because shape (al-shakl) is also an encircling relation not covered. Finally, it addresses a potential counterexample regarding mata (when) and the 'now' (al-an), resolving it by stating that relation to the boundary of time is mediated by time itself.

Key Terms
اعتراض (objection)
نسبة (relation)
كم قار (stationary quantity)
إحاطة (encircling)
أين (where)
متى (when)
مضاف (relative)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the objection completely refutes the enumeration of accidents.
Correction: The commentary shows that the objection can be addressed: the relation by encircling is not exclusive, and cases like shape and the 'now' are still covered.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection raised against Ibn Sina's enumeration?
A) That the division into nine is arbitrary.
B) That the relation to stationary quantity is not limited to encircling.
C) That there are more than ten categories.
D) That quality is not a proper category.

Key Takeaway
The objection that relation to stationary quantity is not limited to encircling is answered by noting that shape is also encircling and that the 'now' case is mediated by time, thus preserving the enumeration.

Defending the Consideration of External Relations in Wad'
Commentator defends adding external relations to wad' by analyzing Ibn Sina's methods and solving an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why later scholars added external relations to the definition of wad' and how this relates to Ibn Sina's two methods for confining categories.

Core Idea
External relations in wad' prevent the categories of standing and reflection from collapsing into the same genus.

This passage from the commentary on the Isharat explains a key debate in Islamic philosophy: whether the category of position (wad') should be defined only by the relation of parts of a body to each other, or also by their relation to external things. Ibn Sina (Avicenna) did not consider external relations, but later scholars added them to avoid conflating distinct categories. We will trace the commentator's argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the gloss on 'فاعتبرت'
The commentator explains that the phrase 'so it was considered' indicates that earlier philosophers considered both internal and external relations in the definition of wad'.
This sets the stage for defending the inclusion of external relations.
2
Examine Ibn Sina's first method
The first method: an accident is either stable in its subject and in need of an external cause (giving quantity, quality, position) or not. This does not involve external relations in wad'.
3
Examine Ibn Sina's second method
The second method: every accident either requires for its conception something external to the subject or does not. Again, no external relations are needed for wad'.
4
Understand why later scholars added external relations
Later scholars introduced external relations to avoid identifying standing (qiyam) with reflection (in'ikas). Without them, the two would be the same in genus.
5
Follow the resolution of the genus-differentia objection
The commentator resolves a potential objection: how can a portion of genus separate from one differentia and join another? He explains that a mental part from external relations acts as a differentia for the mental part from internal relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فاعتبرت) على صيغة المجهول بقرينة قوله كما مر يعني أن القوم اعتبروا في الوضع النسبتين معاً والمقصود بيان الحصر في الأقسام التسعة التي قررها القوم لا التقسيم ابتداء وبيان الحصر في أقسامه فلا يرد أن الشيخ لم يعتبر النسبة إلى الأمور الخارجة في الوضع واعتبار غيره لا يصير حجة عليه فلا يلزم تكثيرُ الأقسام واعلم أنَّ الشيخ نقل أولاً وجه الحصر من فقال : العرض إما أن يكون مستقراً في موضوعه وارد عليه بسبب غيره من خارج ولا محتاجاً إلى النسبة إلى ذلك الخارج وهو أقسام ثلاثة كمية وكيفية ووضع إلخ ثم قال : في وجه الحصر الذي أحدثه أن كل عرض لا يخلو إما أن يحوج تصوره إلى تصور شيء خارج عن الموضوع أو لا يحوج، والذي لا يحوج إلى ذلك على ذلك ثلاثة أقسام : إما أن يكون لم يحوج إلى ذلك فقد يحوج إلى وقوع نسبة في أشياء هي فيه ليست خارجة عنه وإما أن لا يحوج إلى ذلك بالنسبة والذي لا يحوج إلى ذلك فهذه الخارجة تجعل الموضوع منقسماً بوجه ما حتى يكون له أجزاء لبعضها عند بعض حال متغايرة في النسبة وذلك هو مقولة الوضع إذ هو نسبة أجزاء الجسم بعضها إلى بعض وأنَّ كل واحد منها أين هو من الكل إلخ، ولا يخفى أنه ليس على شيء من هذين الوجهين اعتبار النسبة إلى الأمور الخارجة في الوضع ولعل اعتبارها كما أراد المتأخرون لئلا يكون القيام عين الانعكاس لكن اللازم من عدم اعتبارها هو اتحادهما في الجنس لا في النوع فيجوز أن يختلف بالفصول المقومة وما ذكره الشارح قدس سره سابقاً من أنَّ الجنس والفصل متحدان وجوداً وجعلاً فكيف يتصور أن حصة من الجنس قارنت فصلاً ثم فارقته إلى فصل آخر إنما يرد لو قيل : إِنَّ النسبة إلى الأمور الخارجة فصل والنسبة بين الأجزاء جنس بل نقول إنَّ الجزء الذهني المأخوذ من النسبة إلى الأمور الخارجة فصل للجزء الذهني المأخوذ من النسبة بين الأمور الداخلة كالحيوان المأخوذ من البدن والناطق المأخوذ من الصورة النوعية فبعد مفارقته لا تبقى تلك الحصة من الجنس بل تنعدم وإنما تبقى النسبة في الأجزاء التي هي مبدأ لحصة أخرى من الوضع ويقارنها النسبة إلى الأمور الخارجة التي هي مبدأ لفصل آخر . قوله : ( وأيضاً فاعتبرت في الوضع إلخ) أي على المذهب المختار الذي أثبت بالدليل فيما سبق وإن ذهب أبو علي إلى خلافه كما أشير إليه الآن .
His statement: 'so it was considered' in the passive voice, indicated by his saying 'as has passed', meaning that the people (al-qawm) considered in wad' (position) both relations together, and the purpose is to show the confinement (al-hasr) to the nine divisions that the people established, not a beginning division, and to show the confinement in its divisions. So it is not open to objection that the Master (al-Shaykh) did not consider the relation to external things in wad', and the consideration of others does not become a proof against him, so the multiplication of divisions does not follow. Know that the Master first transmitted the aspect of confinement from someone, saying: 'The accident is either stable in its subject, occurring to it due to something else external, and not needing the relation to that external, and it is three divisions: quantity, quality, and position, etc.' Then he said: 'In the aspect of confinement that he introduced: every accident either requires for its conception the conception of something external to the subject or does not require. That which does not require it has three divisions: either it is that which does not require that, and it may require the occurrence of a relation in things that are in it not external to it; or it does not require that by way of relation; and that which does not require that — these external things make the subject divisible in some way so that it has parts, some of which have a state differing in relation, and that is the category of wad', for it is the relation of the parts of a body to each other and that each one of them is located in relation to the whole, etc.' And it is not hidden that in neither of these two aspects is there consideration of the relation to external things in wad'. Perhaps their consideration was as the later scholars intended, so that standing (qiyam) should not be identical to reflection (in'ikas). But the necessary consequence of not considering them is their union in genus, not in species; so it is permissible for them to differ by constitutive differentiate. And what the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) mentioned previously — that genus and species are united in existence and making — so how can it be conceived that a portion of genus accompanied a differentia and then separated from it to another differentia? This only applies if it were said that the relation to external things is a differentia and the relation between parts is a genus. Rather, we say that the mental part taken from the relation to external things is a differentia for the mental part taken from the relation between internal things, like 'animal' taken from the body and 'rational' taken from the specific form. So after its separation, that portion of the genus does not remain but perishes; only the relation in the parts that is a principle for another portion of wad' remains, and it is accompanied by the relation to external things that is a principle for another differentia.' His statement: 'And also it was considered in wad', etc.' — that is, according to the chosen school that was proven by evidence previously, even though Abu Ali went contrary to it, as was indicated now.
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a commentary defending the consideration of external relations in the definition of wad' (position). It begins by explaining a gloss on the phrase 'so it was considered' (فاعتبرت) in the passive voice, indicating that the earlier philosophers (al-qawm) considered both internal and external relations in their definition of wad'. The purpose was to confine the categories to nine, not to establish a new division. Therefore, the objection that Ibn Sina (al-Shaykh) did not consider external relations in wad' is irrelevant, because his method was different. The commentator then presents Ibn Sina's two methods for confining the categories. The first method: an accident is either stable in its subject and in need of an external cause (yielding quantity, quality, and position) or not. The second method: every accident either requires for its conception something external to the subject or does not. Both methods do not involve external relations in wad'. Later scholars introduced external relations to avoid identifying standing (qiyam) with reflection (in'ikas). The commentator argues that ignoring external relations leads to the two being united in genus but different in species. He then addresses a potential objection: if genus and differentia are united in existence, how can a portion of genus separate from one differentia and join another? He resolves this by stating that the mental part from external relations is a differentia for the mental part from internal relations, akin to 'animal' and 'rational'. After separation, the original portion of genus ceases to exist, and a new relation emerges. The passage ends with a gloss confirming that this is the chosen school, as opposed to Abu Ali's (Ibn Sina's) differing view.

Key Terms

النسبة
الكم
الكيف
المقولة
الجنس
الفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That wad' (position) is defined only by internal relations between parts of a body.
Correction: The commentator argues that external relations are also considered, as later scholars added them to avoid identity of standing and reflection.

Checkpoint
How many methods did Ibn Sina present for confining the categories according to the commentator?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
Later scholars added external relations to the definition of wad' to prevent standing from being identical to reflection in genus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study complex theological concepts, do you ever assume that a simpler definition (like wad' only with internal relations) is sufficient? Where in your life do you resist adding nuance because it complicates your mental framework? How might this resistance keep you from seeing reality more accurately?
?
The commentator argues that even a small distinction (like external vs. internal relations) prevents a major confusion (identifying standing with reflection). What is one area of your faith where a clear distinction could protect you from a serious misunderstanding? Are you willing to do the intellectual work to make that distinction, even if it feels tedious?
?
You have just read a sophisticated metaphysical argument about categories and relations. But do you truly believe that such precision matters for your relationship with Allah? Or do you secretly think it is irrelevant? How might your lack of precise understanding about Allah's attributes or creation affect your worship and reliance on Him?

Objection Regarding Relation to Time
First objection: relation to time does not require inherence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the category 'when' (mata) does not necessarily involve the accident being contained in time.

Core Idea
Time's relation to motion and body does not require inherence in time.

The first objection challenges the claim that every accident must fall into one of nine categories. The opponent argues that the relation to time (mata) does not require the accident to be 'in time' as a container. Both motion (which time measures) and body (which is the locus of motion) have a relation to time without being located in it.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Carefully read the Arabic and translation of the first objection. Notice that it targets the category of 'when' (mata).
The objection says: 'It is not necessary that the relation be by occurring in it so that it is when.'
2
Identify the core claim
What is the opponent asserting? That motion and body have a relation to time without being 'in' time. Understand the distinction between 'in time' and 'related to time'.
3
Reflect on the implication
If this objection holds, then the category of 'when' is not necessarily distinct; it might be reducible or in need of redefinition. Ask yourself: Does this undermine the ninefold division?
This is a logical challenge to the necessity of the classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( متى ) إذ لا يجب أن تكون تلك النسبة بالحصول فيه حتى تكون متى ) فإن للحركة ) التي كان الزمان مقدارها ( والجسم) الذي هو محل تلك الحركة (نسبة إلى الزمان وليس انتساب شيء منهما إلى الزمان ( لحصوله فيه قوله : ( هاتين ) أي الفعل والانفعال. وأيضاً لا نسلم أن النسبة إلى الكيف لا تعقل إلا بأنه من غيره أو منه غيره وما الدليل عليه ) بل قد تكون تلك النسبة بالمشابهة وإذا جاز أن تكون النسبة إليه على وجه آخر لم تكن منحصرة في أن يفعل وأن ينفعل على أن انحصار هاتين المقولتين في النسبة إلى الكيف منظور فيه ( وأيضاً فالنسبة إلى ( ذات ( الجوهر معقولة كالحصول فيه ) أعني حلول الأعراض في ذات الجوهر ( وكون الحيز حيزاً له وهو غير حصوله في الحيز) لأن حصوله فيه نسبة له إلى حيزه وكونه حيزاً له نسبة للحيز إليه ) وبالجملة فليس) انتفاء ما أبديناه من الأقسام ( ضروريا وأنتم مطالبون بالحجة) عليه
His statement: 'As for when (mata), since it is not necessary that that relation be by occurring in it so that it is when' — for motion, whose measure is time, and body, which is the locus of that motion, have a relation to time, and it is not that either of them is related to time by occurring in it. His statement: 'these two' means action and passion. 'And also we do not concede that the relation to quality is only conceivable as 'from it' or 'from it' [i.e., acting or being acted upon]; what is the proof for it? Rather, that relation may be by similarity. And if it is permissible for the relation to it to be in another way, it is not confined to acting and being acted upon, especially since the confinement of these two categories to relation to quality is open to scrutiny. And also the relation to the substance itself is conceivable, such as occurrence in it — I mean the inherence of accidents in the substance itself — and being a locus (hāyiz) for it, which is different from its occurrence in the locus, because its occurrence in it is a relation of it to its locus, and its being a locus for it is a relation of the locus to it. In short, the nonexistence of the divisions we have mentioned is not necessary, and you are required to produce proof for it.'
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a series of objections against the claim that there are exactly nine categories of accidents. The commentator explains each objection as it appears in the original text. First, regarding the category of when (mata): the relation to time is not necessarily by being 'in time'; motion and body have a relation to time without being located in it. Second, regarding the relation to quality: it is not limited to action and passion; it could be by similarity (al-mushābaha). The commentator inserts a gloss clarifying that 'these two' refers to action and passion. Third, regarding the relation to substance: it can be conceived as inherence (ḥulūl) of accidents in the substance, or as one thing being a locus (ḥāyiz) for another, which is a different relation. The conclusion is that the absence of these possible divisions is not self-evident, and the opponent must provide a proof. The objection challenges the exhaustiveness of the ninefold classification.

Key Terms
الزمان
متى
النسبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That time is a container for accidents, so every accident is 'in time'.
Correction: The objection shows that motion and body have a relation to time without being contained in it.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what basis is not necessary for an accident to be related to time?
A) That the accident is a substance
B) That the accident occurs in time
C) That time is a measure
D) That the accident is a quality

Key Takeaway
The relation to time does not require an accident to be in time as a container; thus the category 'when' may not be indispensable.
