باطل وإن انتهت إلخ . قوله : (بل هو الاختصاص الناعت سند للمنع المذكور كأنه قيل: لم لا يجوز أن يكون القيام هو الاختصاص الناعت والمراد بالاختصاص الارتباط ونسبة النعت إليه مجازي لكونه سبباً له كما يفصح عنه عبارة الشرح. قوله : ( لأن الكل تابع لذلك الجوهر في تحيزه هذا التعليل يدل على أن مدار الاستدلال الثاني أيضاً كون القيام بمعنى التبعية في التحيز وحينئذ فالجواب: عن الاستدلال الأول بمنع كون معني القيام هو التبعية في التحيز جواب عن الاستدلال الثاني أيضاً لكن لما كان مدارية ما ذكر للاستدلال الثاني غير مذكور في تقرير المصنف صريحاً بخلاف مداريته للأول جعل المنع المذكور جواباً عن الأول فقط، وأما الجواب عن الاستدلال الثاني فهو لا يتوقف على منع المدار بل يكون جواباً وإن سلم أن معنى القيام هو التبعية في التحيز كما لا يخفى . قوله : (بل هو الاختصاص الناعت قال الإمام في المباحث المشرقية فإن قالوا: وما حقيقة ذلك الاختصاص فنقول : إنّه لا طريق لنا إلى معرفة ماهية ذلك الاختصاص إلا بذكر هذا اللازم هذا المرصد الأول المقصد السادس : لا يجوز قيام العرض بالعرض الشيء نعتاً للآخر والآخر منعوتاً به فيسمى الأول حالاً، والثاني محلاً له كاختصاص بالجسم لا كاختصاص الماء بالكوز ( ويحققه) أي يحقق أنّ معنى القيام هذا دون ذلك ( أمران الأول أن التحيز صفة للجوهر قائم به وليس) التحيز متحيزاً ( تبعاً لتحيزه وإلا كان الشيء ) الذي هو التحيز (مشروطاً بنفسه ) إن قلنا بوحدة التحيز القائم بذلك الجوهر إذ لا بد أن يقوم التحيز أولاً بالجوهر حتى يتبعه غيره في التحيز فإذا كان ذلك الغير نفس التحيز فقد اشترط قيامه بالجوهر بقيامه بالجوهر وهو اشتراط الشيء بنفسه ( أو تسلسل ( إن قلنا بتعدد التحيز القائم بالجوهر فيكون قيام كل تحيز به مشروطاً بقيام تحيز آخر به قبله وهكذا إلى ما لا نهاية له. الأمر (الثاني أوصاف الباري تعالى قائمة به كما سنبينه من غير شائبة (تحيز في ذاته وصفاته ( وأما ) الوجه ( الثاني فلأنه لا ينفي أن يقوم عرض بعرض ثان ( وذلك ) العرض الثاني ( بآخر مترتبة إلى أن ينتهي إلى الجوهر) فيكون بعضها تابعاً لذلك الجوهر في تحيزه ابتداء والبعض الآخر تابعاً للبعض الأول وليس يلزم من ذلك كون الكل قائماً بالجوهر ونابعاً له في تحيزه ابتداء بل هناك ما يتبعه في ذلك بواسطة والقول بأن التابع لا يكون قوله : ( وتحققه) إثبات لتكوين معنى القيام الاختصاص الناعت فهو معارضة لكون القيام عبارة عن التحيز فقد صرح المحققون بصحة المعارضة في التعريفات ثم اللازم من الأمرين نفي أن يكون معنى القيام التحيز وأما أن معناه الاختصاص فلا بد من ضم مقدمة وهي أنه لا ثالث فإذا بطل أحدهما تعين الآخر. قوله : ( إنَّ التحيز صفة إلخ) أي عرض قائم بالجوهر لأن الأين من الموجودات العينية باتفاق الحكماء والمتكلمين فما قيل إنَّه أمر اعتباري فلا يلزم أن يكون قيامه عبارة عن التبعية في الحيز ثم الجواب بأنه لا فرق بين قيام العرض والاعتباري وهم . قوله : وهكذا إلى ما لا نهاية (له فيكون للجسم في حيز واحد أكوان غير متناهية والضرورة تكذبه وبرهان التطبيق يبطله . أنه لا فرق بين قيام صفة العلم مثلاً بذاته وبين قيامه بذات قوله : (الأمر الثاني إلخ) يعني الحادث وليس فيه شائبة التحيز أصلاً وتحقيقاً ولا تقديراً فلا يرد أنَّ قيام العرض معناه التحيز لا مطلق القيام حتى يرد النقض بقيام صفاته تعالى بذاته . قوله : ( فلأنه لا ينفي إلخ) يعني أنَّ قولك فالكل قائم به إن أردت به قيام الكل به ابتداء فالملازمة ممنوعة لأنَّ الانتهاء إلى الجوهر لا يستلزم ذلك وإن أردت به قيام الكل به ولو بالواسطة فالملازمة مسلمة لكن بطلان التالي ممنوع لأنه المتنازع فيه . وليس إذا لم يعرف حقيقة الشيء بمقوماته وجب نفي ذلك الشيء فإنَّ أكثر الأشياء إنما تعرف باللوازم .
The First Observatory - Sixth Objective: It is not permissible for an accident to inhere in an accident. ( ( It is only conceivable in what is spatially extended by essence, because what is spatially extended by following something else cannot be followed by a third, since its being followed by that third is not more likely than its being a follower of it. ) And the accident is not spatially extended ) by essence, but rather it is a follower in spatial extension of the substance ( so nothing else inheres in it. The second aspect: The accident that constitutes it ) cannot inhere in itself, and ( if it inheres in another accident, the discussion recurs and the constituting accidents would regress infinitely ( Otherwise, all those regressing accidents would exist without a locus, and you already know its invalidity due to the impossibility of an accident—whether one or multiple—inhering by itself; rather, it must have a locus in which it inheres ( and if the constituting accidents ) terminate ( at the substance, then all ) adhere to it ) because all are followers of that substance in its spatial extension. And thus, no accident inheres in an accident, and the contrary is assumed to be otherwise. ( Both ) i.e., these two aspects are weak. As for the first, we do not grant that inherence is spatial extension, following what you mentioned, ( but rather it is attributive specification, which is that something is specified by another in a specification by which it becomes... ) from one of them due to what is spatially extended by essence and is attributed to what is spatially extended by following, considering that it has a certain relation to what is spatially extended by essence, like the description of the state of the related thing—not that there is one personal spatial extension that inheres in both, and because there are two other spatial extensions—so understand, for feet have slipped in it. His saying: (And the accident is not spatially extended by essence—a second premise for the proof. Its explanation: The followed in spatial extension is spatially extended by essence, and the accident is not spatially extended, so the followed in spatial extension is not an accident. So what is said that this premise is superfluous is an illusion. His saying: (Existing without a locus) because if it existed in a locus, it would not be regressing infinitely. His saying: (And if it terminates) is a conjunction to what is understood from his saying 'and regress', as if it were said: if it does not terminate, regress follows, which is invalid; and if it terminates, etc. His saying: (But it is attributive specification) is the basis for the aforementioned denial, as if it were said: why may it not be that inherence is attributive specification? And by 'specification' is meant connection, and the attribution of the adjective to it is metaphorical because it is a cause for it, as is clarified by the expression of the commentary. His saying: (Because all are followers of that substance in its spatial extension) This reasoning indicates that the pivot of the second inference is also that inherence is in the sense of following in spatial extension. And thus, the answer to the first inference by denying that the meaning of inherence is following in spatial extension is also an answer to the second inference. But because the pivot of what was mentioned for the second inference is not explicitly stated in the author's formulation, unlike its pivoting for the first, the mentioned denial was made an answer only for the first. As for the answer to the second inference, it does not depend on denying the pivot but is an answer even if it is granted that the meaning of inherence is following in spatial extension, as is not hidden. His saying: (But it is attributive specification) The Imam said in al-Mabahith al-Mashriqiyya: If they say, 'What is the reality of that specification?' We say: 'We have no way to know the essence of that specification except by mentioning this necessary concomitant.' This observatory, sixth objective: It is not permissible for an accident to inhere in an accident. The thing is an adjective for the other, and the other is described by it, so the first is called a state (hal), and the second a locus (mahall) for it, like the specification of the body, not like the specification of water in a jug. (And it verifies it) i.e., it verifies that the meaning of inherence is this, not that, through two matters: The first: That spatial extension is an attribute of the substance inhering in it, and spatial extension is not spatially extended (as a follower of its spatial extension), otherwise the thing (i.e., spatial extension) would be conditional upon itself if we say the spatial extension inhering in that substance is one, for spatial extension must first inhere in the substance so that something else may follow it in spatial extension; so if that something else is spatial extension itself, then its inherence in the substance has been conditioned by its inherence in the substance, which is conditioning a thing by itself. (Or there would be regress) if we say the spatial extensions inhering in the substance are multiple, so that the inherence of each spatial extension in it is conditioned by the inherence of another spatial extension before it, and so on ad infinitum. The second matter: The attributes of God Most High inhere in Him, as we will show, without any taint of spatial extension in His essence and attributes. (And as for) the second (aspect), it does not negate that an accident inheres in a second accident, and that second (accident) in a third, arranged until it terminates at the substance. Thus some of them are followers of that substance in its spatial extension directly, and others are followers of the first batch, and it does not follow from that that all are inhering in the substance and following it in its spatial extension directly; rather, there is what follows it in that through mediation. And the statement that the follower cannot be... His saying: (And it verifies it) is a demonstration that the meaning of inherence is attributive specification, so it is a counterargument to the claim that inherence is spatial extension. For the verifiers have affirmed the validity of counterarguments in definitions. Then the necessary consequent from the two matters is to negate that the meaning of inherence is spatial extension. As for its being specification, a premise must be added, which is that there is no third possibility, so if one is invalidated, the other is established. His saying: (Verily, spatial extension is an attribute, etc.) i.e., an accident inhering in the substance, because spatial location (ayn) is among the real existents by agreement of the philosophers and theologians. So what is said that it is a conceptual thing, so its inherence need not be expressed as following in spatial extension, then the answer that there is no difference between the inherence of an accident and a conceptual thing is an illusion. His saying: And thus ad infinitum, so the body would have in one spatial location infinite spatial locations, and common sense belies it and the proof of correspondence refutes it. That there is no difference between the inherence of the attribute of knowledge, for example, in His essence and its inherence in an essence. His saying: (The second matter, etc.) means the contingent, and there is no taint of spatial extension in it essentially, actually, or potentially, so it is not objected that the inherence of an accident means spatial extension, not absolute inherence, so as to be refuted by the inherence of His attributes in His essence. His saying: (For it does not negate, etc.) means that your statement 'so all are inhering in it' - if you mean by it the inherence of all in it directly, then the concomitance is denied, because termination at the substance does not necessitate that. And if you mean by it the inherence of all in it, even mediately, then the concomitance is granted, but the invalidity of the consequent is denied because it is the point of contention. And if the reality of a thing is not known through its constitutive elements, it does not necessitate the denial of that thing, for most things are known only through their necessary concomitants.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage is a continuous refutation and verification concerning a key kalam doctrine: that an accident cannot inhere in another accident. The discussion is dense and proceeds in multiple steps. First, the text presents a second weak argument (الوجه الثاني) for the impossibility: if an accident (عرض) were to inhere in another accident, there would be an infinite regress of accidents, which is impossible. Alternatively, if the regress ends at a substance (جوهر), then all accidents in the chain would inhere in that substance, contradicting the assumption that an accident is inhering in another accident. The commentator notes that both arguments presented are weak (ضعيفان). The refutation begins by denying that inherence (قيام) is equivalent to spatial extension (تحيز). Instead, inherence is defined as 'attributive specification' (اختصاص ناعت): a relation where one thing becomes an attribute of another, not identical to spatial occupancy. This is illustrated by the difference between an attribute inhering in a body (like whiteness in a body) versus water being contained in a jug—the latter is not attributive specification. The passage then provides two positive arguments (أمران) to verify this correct meaning. The first argument shows that if inherence were spatial extension, spatial extension itself would either be conditioned by itself (circular) or lead to regress. The second argument appeals to God's attributes: they inhere in His essence without any spatial extension, proving that inherence is not spatial occupancy. Finally, the commentator addresses the second weak argument directly, clarifying that termination at a substance does not require all to inhere directly; some may inhere through mediation, which is the very point disputed. The passage concludes that not knowing a thing's reality through its constitutive elements does not justify denying it, as most things are known through their necessary concomitants.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a thing's essence is not known through its constitutive elements, it does not exist.
Correction: The passage argues that most things are known through their necessary concomitants, so lack of constitutive definition is not grounds for denial.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that all accidents inhere directly in the substance?
A) By denying the existence of accidents
B) By distinguishing direct and mediated inherence
C) By appealing to divine attributes
D) By arguing for infinite regress

Key Takeaway
Accidents can inhere in other accidents mediatedly, and the unknown essence of a thing does not disprove its existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often encounter theological concepts that you cannot fully grasp. The lesson teaches that not knowing the essence does not negate existence. In what area of your faith—such as Allah’s attributes, destiny, or the afterlife—have you been tempted to reject or downplay a truth because you couldn’t fully understand it? Will you commit today to accepting that truth and seeking knowledge through its signs?
?
The passage distinguishes between direct and mediated inherence. In your relationship with Allah, do you sometimes feel that only direct, unmediated experiences (like prayer without distraction) are valid? How might you be dismissing the value of mediated means—such as scholars, good company, or religious practices—that actually connect you to Him? What is one step you can take to honor those means?
?
The refutation shows that assumptions can lead to false conclusions. Think of a long-held belief you have about Islam or about yourself that might be based on an unexamined assumption—like assuming that if you cannot see or feel a truth, it is not real. How does this lesson challenge you to re-evaluate that assumption, and what concrete action will you take to test it against the revelation?

The Third Proof: Infinite Regress and Its Refutation
Author presents a third proof from infinite regress and refutes it by restricting to different types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof from infinite regress and its refutation by restricting the dispute to accidents of different types.

Core Idea
The infinite regress proof fails because it confuses same-type and different-type inherence.

This lesson presents a third argument against the possibility of an accident inhering in another accident—the argument from infinite regress. The passage also provides the refutation, clarifying that the dispute only concerns accidents of different kinds. By examining this debate, you will see how kalam reasoning carefully defines the scope of claims to avoid invalid conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage contains both a proof and a refutation, along with commentary.
2
Identify the proof and its refutation
The proof claims that if accident-in-accident inherence were allowed, knowledge could inhere in knowledge, leading to infinite regress. Find where the refutation begins and note its core point.
The refutation starts with 'It is refuted by...'
3
Understand the key restriction
The refutation restricts the disputed matter to the inherence of different accidents only. Also note that similarity and opposition are valid impediments, preventing the conjunction of similars or opposites.
This restriction invalidates the infinite regress because it assumes same-type inherence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قد احتج بعضهم بوجه ثالث فقال : لو جاز قيام العرض بالعرض لجاز قيام العلم بالعلم، ثم الكلام في العلم القائم بالعلم كالكلام في العلم الأول فيلزم التسلسل وهو مردود بأنَّ المتنازع فيه قيام بعض الأعراض المختلفة. قوله : ( والقول إلخ ) جواب سؤال مقدر لا يخفى تقريره . قوله : ( لجاز قيام العلم إلخ إذ لا فرق بين عرض وعرض في جواز قيام أحدهما بالعرض دون الآخر فلا يرد أن الملازمة ممنوعة لأن الخصم لم يدع جواز قيام كل عرض بكل عرض . قوله : (قيام بعض الأعراض إلخ) لأنَّ المراد أنه هل يجوز قيام العرض بالعرض إذا لم يوجد مانع آخر والتماثل والتضاد مانع لأنه يلزم اجتماع المثلين والمتضادين.
Some people argued with a third proof, saying: If it were permissible for an accident to inhere in an accident, then it would be permissible for knowledge to inhere in knowledge. Then the discourse concerning the knowledge inhering in knowledge is like the discourse concerning the first knowledge, so an infinite regress follows. It is refuted by [the fact] that the subject of dispute is the inherence of some different accidents. His saying: 'And the statement etc.' is the answer to an implied question, whose formulation is clear. His saying: 'It would be permissible for knowledge to inhere etc.' [means] because there is no difference between one accident and another in the permissibility of one inhering in the other without the other, so it cannot be denied that the implication [holds], for the opponent did not claim the permissibility of every accident in every accident. His saying: 'Inherence of some accidents etc.' [means] because the intended [meaning] is whether it is permissible for an accident to inhere in an accident when no other impediment exists, and similarity and opposition are impediments because they entail the conjunction of similars and opposites.
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage presents a third proof against the possibility of an accident inhering in an accident, followed by its refutation and a commentary clarifying the refutation. The proof claims that if such inherence were allowed, then knowledge could inhere in knowledge, leading to an infinite regress. The refutation restricts the disputed topic to accidents of different types, not identical ones. The commentary explains that the opponent does not claim that every accident can inhere in every accident; similarity and opposition are valid impediments, thus the proof is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the refutation concedes that same-type inherence is possible.
Correction: The refutation actually restricts the dispute to accidents of different types, so it does not concede same-type inherence.

Checkpoint
Why does the refutation in the third proof deny the possibility of infinite regress?
A) Because the opponent concedes that knowledge cannot inhere in knowledge.
B) Because the dispute is limited to accidents of different types, not identical ones.
C) Because infinite regress is impossible in all cases.
D) Because knowledge is not a real accident.

Key Takeaway
The third proof from infinite regress fails because it assumes inherence of identical accidents, whereas the dispute concerns accidents of different kinds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that infinite regress is avoided by distinguishing types. In your own life, do you ever justify wrong actions by claiming they are just like other permissible actions, ignoring crucial differences? Reflect on one area where you have blurred lines that should remain distinct. Commit today to clarifying that distinction in your heart and practice.
?
The opponent's argument fails because it assumes all accidents are alike. Are there areas in your understanding of Allah's attributes where you erroneously assume similarity between His attributes and created qualities? Have you ever compared Allah's knowledge to human knowledge in a way that reduces His transcendence? Spend today affirming His absolute uniqueness (tanzih) in one attribute.
?
The refutation here shows that a valid argument can be misapplied if its scope is misunderstood. In your own spiritual journey, have you ever rejected a truth because you applied a valid reasoning to the wrong domain? Ask Allah to guide you to the correct application of reason in matters of faith, and take one step today to align your understanding with sound theology.

Speed, Slowness, and the Inherence of Accidents
Author presents the philosophers' proof using speed and slowness and refutes it from two perspectives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that accidents can inhere in accidents based on speed and slowness, and the two-part refutation from the Ash'ari perspective and from the philosophers' own principles.

Core Idea
Speed and slowness are properties of the body, not accidents of motion.

The philosophers claim that speed and slowness—accidents describing motion (itself an accident)—demonstrate that accidents can inhere in accidents. This seemingly clever argument is dismantled by the author with two distinct refutations, one rooted in Ash'ari ontology of rests and another challenging the philosophers' own framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Note the philosophers’ claim: speed and slowness are accidents inhering in motion, which is an accident inhering in the body.
Pay attention to the phrase 'قيام العرض بالعرض' – this is the central issue.
2
Understand the Ash'ari refutation
Identify how the author refutes the argument from the Ash'ari school: speed and slowness are not real accidents; they are reducible to the number of intervening rests (سكنات) of the body. Thus they are attributes of the body, not of motion.
The commentary explains that rests are attributes of the body (الأكوان), and speed/slowness result from the quantity of rests.
3
Understand the second refutation
Identify the second refutation from the philosophers' own standpoint: different speeds may be essentially different types of motion, so speed and slowness are not additional accidents but merely the motion itself. The author questions their external existence.
This challenges the philosophers' assumption that speed and slowness are real external attributes.
4
Review the commentary clarifications
Read the commentary carefully, especially the clarifications about ‘لام التعليل’ and the misunderstanding that speed/slowness are the rests themselves. Understand the role of the commentator (الشارح).
The commentary distinguishes between the text’s wording and the commentator’s explanation.

احتج الفلاسفة على جواز قيام العرض بالعرض بأن السرعة والبطء عرضان قائمان بالحركة القائمة بالجسم فإنها توصف بهما فيقال حركة سريعة وحركة بطيئة دون الجسم فإنه ما لم يلاحظ حركته لم يصح بالضرورة أن يوصف بأنه سريع أو بطيء والجواب أنه لا يصح هذا الاحتجاج لا على مذهبنا فإنهما ليسا عرضين بل هما للسكنات المتخللة وقلتها وكثرتها فحاصل البطء أن الجسم يسكن سكنات كثيرة في زمان قطعه المسافة وحاصل السرعة أنه يسكن سكنات قليلة بالقياس إلى سكنات البطء ولا شك أنهما بهذين المعنيين من صفات الجسم المتحرك دون الحركة ولا على مذهبهم لجواز أن تكون طبقات الحركات أنواعاً مختلفة بالحقيقة وليس ثمة أمر موجود إلا الحركة المخصوصة. قوله : (بل) هما للسكنات إلخ) حمل اللام على التعليل على خلاف ما في قوله للحركة لأنَّ السرعة والبطء ليسا عارضين للسكنات بل للجسم ولقوله وقلتها وكثرتها فإنه نص في التعليل والمقصود أن السرعة والبطء عارضان للجسم لأجل السكنات وتفاوت درجاتها لأجل قلتها وكثرتها كما بينه الشارح قدس سره بقوله : يسكن سكنات فإنَّ السكون صفة الجسم أعني الأكوان سبب لكونه ساكناً كما أن الحركات سبب لكونه متحركاً فما قيل : إنَّ عبارة الشرح تفيد أن السرعة والبطء نفس السكنات وعبارة المتن يفيد أنهما لأجل السكنات وهم . قوله : ( لجواز أنَّ تكون إلخ) لا يخفى أن كون مراتب الحركات مختلفة الحقيقة لا دخل له الجواب فإنَّ خلاصته منع كون السرعة والبطء موجودين في الخارج لم لا يجوز أن يكون من الأمور الاعتبارية التي يجوز اتصاف الأعراض بها ؟ وإنما تعرض له ليظهر وجه اختلاف مراتبها فيهما ظهوراً تاماً بخلاف ما إذا كانت مراتبها متفقة فإن اختلافها في السرعة والبطء يحتاج إلى القول بأن ذلك لاختلاف أشخاصها وأنَّ السرعة والبطء أمر زائد على تشخصها .
The philosophers argued for the permissibility of an accident inhering in an accident by [saying] that speed and slowness are two accidents inhering in motion which inheres in the body, for motion is described by them, so it is said 'fast motion' and 'slow motion', not the body, because unless its motion is considered, it is not correct necessarily to describe it as fast or slow. The answer is that this argument is not valid according to our school, for they [speed and slowness] are not two accidents, but rather they are due to the intervening rests and their fewness and manyness. The essence of slowness is that the body rests many rests in the time of traversing the distance, and the essence of speed is that it rests few rests in relation to the rests of slowness. There is no doubt that they, in these two meanings, are attributes of the moving body, not of motion. Nor is it valid according to their school, because it is possible that the layers of motions are different in reality, and there is no existing thing except the particular motion. His saying: 'Rather they [speed and slowness] are due to the rests etc.' [means] the lam is taken for causation, contrary to what is in his saying 'for motion', because speed and slowness are not accidents of the rests but of the body, and because of his saying 'and their fewness and manyness', for it is explicit in causation. The intended meaning is that speed and slowness are accidents of the body on account of the rests, and their degrees differ on account of their fewness and manyness, as the commentator (may his secret be sanctified) explained by his saying: 'It rests rests', for rest is an attribute of the body, namely the modes, a cause for it being at rest, just as motions are a cause for it being moving. So what was said, that the wording of the commentary indicates that speed and slowness are the rests themselves and the wording of the text indicates that they are due to the rests, is a misunderstanding. His saying: 'It is possible that they be etc.' it is not hidden that the fact that the degrees of motions are different in reality has no bearing on the answer, for its essence is the denial that speed and slowness exist externally. What is the impediment to their being among the mental considerations through which accidents may be described? Rather he mentioned it to show fully the aspect of the difference of their degrees in them, unlike the case when their degrees are identical, for their difference in speed and slowness would then require the claim that it is due to the difference of their individuals and that speed and slowness are something additional to their individuation.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' argument that speed and slowness are accidents inhering in motion (an accident), thus proving that accidents can inhere in accidents. The author then gives a two-part refutation. First, according to the Ash'ari school (مذهبنا), speed and slowness are not real accidents; they are merely consequences of the number of intervening rests (سكنات) in the body's motion. Second, even according to the philosophers' own school, it is possible that the different degrees of motion are essentially different types, so speed and slowness are not additional accidents but rather the motion itself. The commentary clarifies that speed and slowness are accidents of the body, not of motion, and that the refutation rests on denying their external existence, suggesting they are mental considerations.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the Ash'ari answer concedes that speed and slowness are accidents of motion.
Correction: In fact, the Ash'ari school reduces speed and slowness to properties of the body (number of rests), so they are not accidents of motion at all.

Checkpoint
How does the Ash'ari school refute the philosophers' argument that speed and slowness are accidents inhering in motion?
A) By denying that motion is an accident.
B) By arguing that speed and slowness are not real accidents but are due to intervening rests in the body.
C) By claiming that speed and slowness are attributes of the motion itself.
D) By accepting the argument but limiting it to specific motions.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' argument that speed and slowness prove accidents can inhere in accidents fails because speed and slowness are not real accidents of motion—they are either properties of the body (due to rests) or the motion itself (if motion types differ).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own life, do you tend to attribute 'speed' or 'slowness' to events as if these were objective realities? How does this lesson's reduction of speed and slowness to the body's rests challenge your perception of cause and effect? Will you now pause before labeling anything as 'fast' or 'slow' and instead reflect on the underlying divine decree that creates each moment?
?
The philosophers argued from common linguistic usage—'fast motion'—to assert a metaphysical reality. Where in your own belief system might you be mistaking a convenient way of speaking for an absolute truth? What would it look like to scrutinize that assumption today, and what change in your worship or worldview might it demand?
?
This lesson shows that what seems to be an independent property (speed) is actually dependent on something else (rests). Can you identify an area of your life—perhaps your productivity, patience, or progress—where you treat a secondary quality as self-sufficient? How will you remind yourself daily that all such qualities are merely manifestations of Allah's creation of moments and rests?

Logic and Technical Terms

15 min
Refuting Accident-on-Accident Subsistence: The Speed and Slowness Argument
Al-Iji refutes the philosophical argument for accident-on-accident subsistence using speed and slowness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari refutation of the philosophers' argument that accidents can subsist in other accidents, using the examples of speed/slowness and roughness/smoothness.

Core Idea
Accident-on-accident is impossible; speed and slowness are relational, not real inhering accidents.

In this passage, al-Iji presents the sixth objective of the first observatory, which denies the possibility of an accident subsisting in another accident. The philosophers attempt to prove such subsistence using speed and slowness as accidents of motion. Al-Iji responds with a two-part refutation, drawing on both Ash'ari principles and the philosophers' own framework, and extends the argument to roughness and smoothness.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' argument
Carefully read the opening section where the philosophers argue that speed and slowness are accidents subsisting in motion, an accident, thus establishing accident-on-accident.
Notice the structure: they give an example, then conclude permissibility.
2
Identify the Ash'ari response
The first part of the answer: speed and slowness are not accidents of motion but are due to the rests (sakanāt) interposed between movements, making them attributes of the body.
The key phrase is 'بل هما للسكنات'.
3
Study the alternative response on their school
The second part grants the philosophers' premises but shows that speed and slowness can be considered relative notions or different types of motion, avoiding real subsistence.
This part concludes that speed and slowness are 'umūr nisbiyya' (relative matters).
4
Examine the additional arguments
The passage then discusses a subtle argument involving blackness and individuation, and the example of roughness and smoothness, further reinforcing the impossibility of accident-on-accident.
These sections strengthen the refutation through different metaphysical angles.

إلى ) حركة ( أخرى ) وعلى هذا فالسرعة والبطء وصفان للحركة اعتباريان ولا نزاع في وصف الأعراض بالأمور الاعتبارية إنما الكلام في وصفها بأمور موجودة وللحكماء احتجاج أنَّ الخشونة والملاسة عرضان من مقولة أمر موهوم عندهم كما سيأتي فإطلاق الموجود عليها باعتبار أنها يتخيل من أمر موجود كما سيأتي .
The first observatory - the sixth objective: It is not permissible for an accident to subsist in an accident. ... the philosophers argued for the permissibility of an accident subsisting in an accident by [saying] that speed and slowness are two accidents subsisting in motion which subsists in the body, for motion is described by them, so it is said 'fast motion' and 'slow motion', not the body, for as long as its motion is not observed, it is not necessarily correct to describe it as fast or slow. The answer: this argument is not valid. According to our school [Ash'ari], indeed they—I mean speed and slowness—are not two accidents subsisting in motion, but rather they are due to the rests interposed between motions and their fewness and multiplicity. So the result of slowness is that the body rests many rests in the time of its traversing the distance, and the result of speed is that it rests few rests compared to the rests of slowness. There is no doubt that they, in these two meanings, are attributes of the moving body, not of motion. And not according to their school [philosophers], because it is possible that the layers of motions and their degrees varying in speed and slowness are different types in reality, and there is no existing thing except the particular motion which is a type of... His saying: 'Rather they are due to the rests, etc.' The lām is taken as causal contrary to what is in his saying 'for motion' because speed and slowness are not accidents of the rests but of the body, and because of his saying 'and their fewness and multiplicity' which is textually causal. The intended meaning is that speed and slowness are accidents of the body due to the rests and the variation of their degrees due to their fewness and multiplicity, as the commentator explained. His saying: 'it is possible that they be, etc.' It is not hidden that the layers of motions being different in reality has no bearing on the answer, for the essence of the answer is the denial that speed and slowness exist externally; why may they not be among the considerational matters by which accidents may be described? He only mentioned it to show the complete manifestation of the aspect of the variation of their degrees in them, unlike if their degrees were uniform, for then their variation in speed and slowness would require saying that it is due to the difference of their individuals and that speed and slowness are something additional to their individuation. Since when the mode of inherence like blackness, for example, is a complete cause for the specific individuation, namely the blackness inhering in that locus, so if a blackness inheres in that blackness, it would necessarily be individuated by the individuation of the first blackness due to the existence of the complete cause for the first individuation, so duality would be negated. And a similar proof invalidates that a blackness should subsist in a locus of sweetness and through this sweetness another blackness, otherwise the distinction between the two blacknesses—the locus for sweetness and the one inhering in it—would be negated due to the realization of the complete cause for the individuation of the first blackness in the second blackness. For if the negation of the locus of sweetness for blackness is considered in the individuation of the first blackness, then let the negation of the individuation of one of the two similars be considered in the cause of that specific individuation, so the elevation of duality would not follow in the subsistence of one of the two similars in the other either. So reflect, for it is subtle. His saying: 'types different in reality' The mention of the difference of the realities of the layers of motions only benefits those different realities. And as for speed and slowness by which motions are described, they are among relative matters which have no external existence. For when the motions different in reality are conceived and some are compared to others, speed and slowness occur to them in the mind. And therefore, because they are two relative matters, the state of motion differs in them according to the difference of comparison, for it—i.e. motion—is fast relative to one motion and slow relative to another motion. On this basis, speed and slowness are two considerational attributes of motion. There is no dispute regarding the description of accidents by considerational matters; the discussion is only about describing them by existing matters. And the philosophers had an argument that roughness and smoothness are two accidents from the category of quality subsisting in the surface, because it is what is described by them, and the surface is an accident. So he indicated his answer by his saying: 'As for roughness and smoothness, if it is conceded that they are two qualities'—i.e. we do not concede that they are from the category of quality; rather they are from the category of position which is considerational relations. And if it is conceded that they are two existing qualities, then their subsistence in the body is from another, and it is by the surface. His saying: 'from the category of quality subsisting, etc.' Because they are among the qualities specific to quantities. His saying: 'from the category of position' because they are two expressions for the equality of parts or their difference by lowness and height, and from this it is known that position does not consider relation to external matters. It might be imagined that the answer could be formulated in another way: that the layers of motions are different types distinguished from each other by speed and slowness, so they would be essential to motions, and the essential of a thing does not subsist in it because it is prior to it in essence, while that which subsists in it is posterior to it. But according to the author's formulation, it does not have much benefit for the intended purpose. And what is said that the mention of it is to prevent the imagination that speed and slowness are causes for the distinction of motions externally, so they would have to exist—that is not heeded, because distinction by reality does not preclude distinction by accident either, so the imagination is not repelled. Then distinction externally may be established from something non-existent in it, like blindness, if it suffices in it the external characterization of the distinguished by this cause externally. There remains here another discussion: that the understood from his speech is that speed and slowness are two considerational matters and the described by them exists externally, but you are aware that the described by them is motion in the sense of traversal which is a suppositional matter according to them, as will come. So calling it 'existing' is based on its being imagined from an existing thing, as will come.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth objective, which denies that an accident can subsist in another accident. The structure is an objection-and-answer format. First, the philosophers' argument is given: speed and slowness are accidents that clearly inhere in motion (an accident) because we say 'fast motion' and 'slow motion', not 'fast body' unless we consider its motion. This would show accident-on-accident. The answer is divided into two parts. On the Ash'ari school (our school), speed and slowness are not actual accidents of motion; rather they are relational properties deriving from the number of rests (سكنات) interposed between movements. Slowness means many rests, speed means few rests. Thus they are attributes of the body, not of motion. On the philosophers' own principles, it is possible that the different layers of motion (fast, slow) are really different types, and speed and slowness are merely relative notions existing in the mind, not extramental accidents. Then the commentary elaborates on these two answers. It clarifies that the lām in 'للسكنات' is causal: speed and slowness are caused by the rests, not inhering in them. The commentator rejects the idea that the text implies speed and slowness are identical to the rests. Then the commentary addresses a subtlety about individuation: if a blackness inheres in another blackness, the two would become identical because the same complete cause of individuation applies; this supports the impossibility of accident-on-accident. The discussion then moves to the philosophers' further example of roughness and smoothness as accidents of the surface (an accident). The answer is that either roughness and smoothness are not truly qualities but from the category of position (وضع), which is relational, or if they are conceded as real qualities, they still subsist in the body via the surface, not directly in the surface as an accident. Finally, the commentary addresses a possible objection: if speed and slowness are essential to motion types, they cannot subsist in motion because the essential is prior. The author's own formulation avoids this. The passage concludes by noting that motion itself (as traversal) is a suppositional concept according to the philosophers, so calling it 'existing' is only metaphorical.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Speed and slowness are real accidents that inhere in motion, proving that accidents can subsist in accidents.
Correction: Speed and slowness are either due to rests in the body (Ash'ari) or are relative mental considerations (philosophers' premises); they do not require real accident-on-accident subsistence.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari answer in the sixth objective, what are speed and slowness actually due to?
A) They are real accidents subsisting in motion.
B) They are attributes of the body arising from the number of rests.
C) They are essential properties of the different layers of motion.
D) They are mental considerations with no basis in reality.

Key Takeaway
Accidents cannot subsist in accidents; apparent examples like speed/slowness and roughness/smoothness are explained through relational notions or underlying substances, not direct inherence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that speed and slowness are not real features of motion but mere comparisons. When you judge someone's actions as 'fast' or 'slow' in your daily life, are you basing that on objective reality or on your own limited perspective? How does this affect your understanding of truth and certainty?
?
If accidents cannot subsist in accidents, then every quality you perceive must ultimately depend on a substance that itself depends on God. Do you live with the conscious awareness that every attribute of this world points directly to its Creator, or do you treat these qualities as self-sufficient?
?
The blackness argument shows that if an accident were to inhere in another accident, they would collapse into one. This reveals the impossibility of independence in the created order. Are there areas of your life where you act as if something is independent of God—whether your wealth, health, or status? Take a moment to recommit every attribute to Allah alone.

Accidents Do Not Persist: The Ash'ari View
Al-Iji states the Ash'ari doctrine that accidents are non-enduring.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari doctrine that accidents are non-enduring and require constant recreation at each moment.

Core Idea
All accidents are new every moment; none lasts two instants.

This lesson introduces a core Ash'ari principle about the nature of accidents. According to al-Ash'ari and his verifying followers, no accident endures for two consecutive moments. Instead, accidents are continuously passing away and being renewed, which underpins their occasionalist worldview.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note that this is a concise doctrinal statement.
The passage is short but foundational for Ash'ari atomism.
2
Understand the claim
The key claim is that accidents do not 'remain' (لا يبقى) for two moments. Ask yourself: what does 'remaining' mean here? It means persisting identically across time.
Accidents are like properties (color, sound) that inhere in substances.
3
Grasp the implication
Since accidents do not endure, they must be recreated by God at each moment. This leads to constant renewal (التجدد) and passing away (التقصي).
This contrasts with the view that some accidents are stable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ المقصد السابع : أن العرض لا يبقي زمانين ] وذهب الشيخ الأشعري ومتبعوه من محققي الأشاعرة إلى أن العرض لا يبقي زمانين فالأعراض جملتها غير باقية عندهم بل هي على التقصي والتجدد ينقضي
[ The seventh objective: That the accident does not remain for two moments ] And al-Ash'ari and his followers among the verifying Ash'aris went to [the position] that the accident does not remain for two moments, so all accidents according to them are non-enduring; rather they are in a state of passing away and renewal, they pass away.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh objective, which is a simple doctrinal statement. It asserts the Ash'ari position that no accident (عرض) persists for two consecutive moments of time. According to al-Ash'ari and the verifying Ash'aris, all accidents are continuously passing away and being replaced; they do not endure. This is a fundamental principle of Ash'ari atomism: accidents are created anew at every moment, which underpins their occasionalist ontology.

Key Terms
العرض
البقاء
الزمان
التجدد
التقصي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some accidents, like motion or color, might persist for a while.
Correction: The Ash'ari view, as stated here, holds that no accident endures for even two moments; all are constantly recreated.

Checkpoint
According to the seventh objective, what is the view of al-Ash'ari and his followers regarding accidents?
A) Accidents persist for two moments.
B) Accidents are eternal.
C) Accidents do not remain for two moments and are constantly renewed.
D) Accidents persist only in the mind.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ash'ari and his followers maintain that accidents do not endure for two moments; they are perpetually passing away and being renewed by God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every accident in your life—every breath, every heartbeat, every thought—is created anew by God each moment, what does that imply about your own sense of control and self-reliance? How should this change the way you begin each day?
?
The Ash'ari view teaches that nothing in the created world—not even the sound of your voice—lasts for two moments. When you sin, that sin is a non-enduring accident. Does that diminish the guilt of sin? Or does it magnify the fact that God chooses to recreate that sin in you each moment? Reflect on the weight of each moment in light of this.
?
If your very existence is sustained by God's continuous creation, what does that mean for your gratitude? How would you live differently today knowing that without God's constant renewal, you would cease to be? What specific aspect of your life have you taken for granted that is actually being recreated right now?

Al-Nazzam's Doctrine: Body as Aggregate of Accidents
Al-Nazzam states his view that the body is a collection of accidents, and explains the implication for renewal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Nazzam's view that the body is a collection of accidents and how this leads to the conclusion that accident renewal entails body renewal.

Core Idea
For al-Nazzam, the body is nothing but aggregated accidents; thus if accidents renew, the body renews.

Al-Nazzam held a unique view that the body is composed solely of aggregated accidents. This lesson introduces his doctrine and explains why he believed that the renewal of accidents necessarily implies the renewal of the body. The passage also distinguishes between different types of accidents and clarifies what constitutes the body's reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that al-Nazzam's view is presented as a lemma from al-Mawaqif.
2
Identify al-Nazzam's definition of body
The passage states that the body is a collection of accidents that remain accidental but become bodies through aggregation. Find the exact wording.
This is the foundation of his doctrine.
3
Understand the implication of accident renewal
If the body is nothing but accidents, then the renewal of accidents necessarily renews the body. Ask yourself: Is this a valid conclusion?
This leads to the need for proof extension discussed later.
4
Notice the distinction among accidents
The passage clarifies that some things (modes, beliefs) are accidents with no role in the body's reality, while others (colors, tastes) are considered subtle bodies by al-Nazzam. Reflect on why this distinction matters.
This shows the complexity of al-Nazzam's category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (يلزم من تجدد الأعراض تجدد (الأجسام لأنَّ المستفاد من قوله أن الجسم مجموع أعراض مجتمعة أنها باقية على عروضيتها وصارت بسبب الاجتماع أجساماً فيكون تجددها موجباً لتجددها هذا لكن في شرح التجريد أن المذكور في كتب المعتزلة أن مثل الأكوان والاعتقادات والآلام واللذات وما أشبه ذلك أعراض لا دخل لها في حقيقة الجسم وفاقاً وأما الألوان والطعوم والروائح والأصوات والكيفيات الملموسة من الحرارة والبرودة وغيرهما فعن النظام جواهر بل أجسام حيث صرح بأنَّ كلاً من ذلك لطيف مركب من جواهر مجتمعة ثم إنَّ تلك الأجسام اللطيفة إذا اجتمعت وتداخلت صارت الجسم الكثيف الذي هو الجماد . :قوله : ( بلا حاجة إلى طرد الدليل لعل المراد من طرد الدليل إجراؤها في جزئي من جزئيات ما أقيم عليه لخفاء فيه كما سيجيء في التنبيه المذكور في المقصد الثالث الأجسام باقية حيث قال : الدليل لما قام في الأعراض طرده النظام في الأجسام فقال بعدم بقائها أيضا قال الآمدي : وذلك لأنه مبني على أصله وهو أنَّ الجوهر مركب من الأعراض انتهى فعلى هذا لزوم تجدد الأجسام من تجدد الأعراض لا ينفي الاحتياج إلى الطرد. قوله : ( وإنما يحتاج إليه إذا كانت الأجسام عنده مركبة من الجواهر الأفراد فيه بحث لأن تركب الجسم من الجواهر الأفراد الغير المتناهية وإن كان مشهوراً من مذهبه إلا أن الجوهر الفرد بل الجوهر مطلقاً مركب عنده من محض الأعراض المجتمعة كما سيجيء في موقف الجوهر فالأجزاء الغير المتناهية عنده جواهر غير متناهية مركب كل منها من الأعراض المجتمعة ولا فرق في الاحتياج المذكور وعدمه بين القولين لأن مبنى نفي الاحتياج على تقدير كون الجسم مجموع الأعراض المجتمعة هو أنَّ المركب من محض الأعراض يلزم أن يكون عرضاً كما يشهد به البديهة وإن كان جوهراً عند النظام فالدليل الدال على تجدد الأعراض دال على تجدد الجسم لاندراجه فيها فلا احتياج إلى طرد الدليل فيه كما لا احتياج إلى طرده في خصوصيات الأعراض وهذا اللزوم لا يختلف على القولين كما لا يخفى على المتأمل اللهم إلا أن يثبت عنه نقل آخر وهو القول بالجزء على نحو ما قال به سائر المتكلمين، وإن لم يذكره المصنف وبما ذكرنا يظهر أن عدم الاحتياج إلى طرد الدليل على ما يشهد به البديهة لا على ما ادعاه النظام من جوهرية مذهبه
His statement: (It follows from the renewal of accidents the renewal of bodies) because what is understood from his statement that the body is a collection of accidents is that they (the accidents) remain upon their accidentally and by aggregation become bodies, so their renewal necessitates their renewal. However, in Sharh al-Tajrid it is mentioned that what is recorded in the books of the Mu'tazila is that things like modes (akwan), beliefs, pains, pleasures, and the like are accidents that have no entry in the reality of the body by agreement. As for colors, tastes, smells, sounds, and tangible qualities like heat and cold and others, according to al-Nazzam they are substances (jawahir) rather bodies (ajsam), for he explicitly stated that each of these is a subtle thing composed of aggregated substances, and then those subtle bodies when they aggregate and interpenetrate become the dense body which is the inanimate. His statement: (without need to extend the proof (sard al-dalil)) perhaps the meaning of 'extending the proof' is applying it to the particulars of the things for which the proof was established, due to obscurity therein, as will come in the notice mentioned in the third objective: bodies are enduring. For he said: The proof when it was established for accidents, al-Nazzam extended it to bodies and said they also do not endure. Al-Amidi said: That is because it is built upon his principle – that the substance (jawhar) is composed of accidents. End of quote. On this basis, the necessity of bodies' renewal from the renewal of accidents does not negate the need for extending the proof. His statement: (And it is only needed if bodies in his view are composed of individual atoms (jawahir afrad).) There is discussion in it, because composition of the body from infinite individual atoms – though it is well-known from his doctrine – nevertheless the individual atom, nay substance absolutely, is in his view composed of mere aggregated accidents, as will come in the station of substance. So the infinite parts are in his view infinite substances each composed of aggregated accidents. There is no difference between the two views concerning the aforementioned need or lack thereof, because the basis for negating the need on the assumption that the body is a collection of aggregated accidents is that a composite of mere accidents necessarily is an accident, as intuition (badiha) testifies, even if it is a substance according to al-Nazzam. So the proof indicating the renewal of accidents indicates the renewal of the body because it falls under them, so there is no need to extend the proof to it, just as there is no need to extend it to specifics of accidents. And this implication does not differ between the two views, as is not hidden from the contemplator. Unless there is established from him another transmitted statement – namely the saying of the part (al-juz') in the manner spoken by the rest of the theologians, even if the author did not mention it. And by what we have mentioned it becomes apparent that the lack of need for extending the proof is according to what intuition testifies, not according to what al-Nazzam claimed of the substantiality of his doctrine.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a lemma from al-Mawaqif regarding the implication of accident renewal for body renewal under al-Nazzam's doctrine. It begins by explaining the lemma: if the body is a collection of accidents (as al-Nazzam held), then the renewal of accidents necessarily entails the renewal of the body, because the body is nothing but those accidents. However, it notes an alternative from Sharh al-Tajrid: the Mu'tazila agreed that certain things (e.g., modes, beliefs, pains) are accidents not constitutive of the body, but colors, tastes, etc. were considered by al-Nazzam to be subtle bodies that aggregate to form dense bodies. The passage then discusses the phrase 'without need to extend the proof' (sard al-dalil). It explains that extending the proof means applying it to the particulars of the things for which the proof was originally established (here, accidents) due to some obscurity. It recounts that al-Nazzam extended the proof from accidents to bodies, claiming bodies also do not endure. Al-Amidi says this is because al-Nazzam's principle is that the substance (jawhar) is composed of accidents. So the renewal of accidents does not by itself negate the need to extend the proof. The passage then examines the condition 'if bodies are composed of individual atoms (jawahir afrad)'. Despite this being famed as al-Nazzam's view, the commentator argues that for al-Nazzam even the individual atom is composed of mere aggregated accidents (as will be explained later). Therefore, the need for extending the proof is the same on both views (body as aggregate of accidents vs. body as composed of atoms) because the basis for negating the need is that a composite of mere accidents is itself an accident by intuition. So the proof of accident renewal automatically includes the body, and extension is unnecessary. The passage concludes that the lack of need is based on intuitive reason, not on al-Nazzam's claim of substantiality. It leaves open the possibility of another transmitted view (the part) but says the author did not mention it. The argument shows the theological subtlety of how different views of body composition affect the application of proofs.

Key Terms
عرض
جوهر
الجماد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All accidents are equally aspects of the body's reality.
Correction: Al-Nazzam, like other Mu'tazila, held that some accidents (e.g., modes, beliefs) do not enter into the body's reality, while others (colors, tastes) are considered subtle bodies.

Checkpoint
According to al-Nazzam, what are colors and tastes?
A) Accidents that have no entry in the body's reality
B) Substances (jawahir) or subtle bodies
C) Modes (akwan)
D) Individual atoms (jawahir afrad)

Key Takeaway
Al-Nazzam's doctrine that the body is an aggregate of accidents leads to the conclusion that accident renewal automatically entails body renewal, but this is based on his unique view of what constitutes a substance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your body is nothing but a collection of continuously renewing accidents, what part of 'you' remains constant? How does that affect your understanding of accountability for actions done in a body that is never the same from moment to moment? Will you now pay more attention to the intentions of your heart, which may not be subject to such renewal?
?
Al-Nazzam saw physical properties like colors and tastes as subtle bodies that interpenetrate. Do you view your own physical sensations as merely temporary or as part of your enduring self? How does this perspective influence the seriousness you give to physical worship acts like prayer or fasting? Commit to observing one physical act of worship this week with the awareness that it is composed of renewing accidents.
?
The lesson distinguishes between accidents that do not enter into the reality of the body and those that do (according to al-Nazzam). What in your life do you consider 'accidental' and 'essential' to your identity? Is there a trait you consider essential that might actually be accidental? Challenge yourself to ask whether your faith is an accident or substance in your life.

The Concept of Extending the Proof (Sard al-Dalil)
Defines sard al-dalil and discusses its application in al-Nazzam's argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what is meant by 'extending the proof' (sard al-dalil) in kalam reasoning and why al-Nazzam's extension from accidents to bodies is significant.

Core Idea
Extending the proof means applying a general proof to its particulars; al-Nazzam extended the accident proof to bodies.

In this lesson, we explore the concept of 'extending the proof' (sard al-dalil). The passage explains that when a proof is established for a general category, it may need to be extended to its particulars if there is obscurity. Al-Nazzam extended the proof of the non-endurance of accidents to bodies, claiming bodies also do not endure. This lesson unpacks that logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and find the definition of sard al-dalil
The passage says: 'perhaps the meaning of extending the proof is applying it to the particulars of the things for which the proof was established, due to obscurity therein.' Locate this definition.
This is a technical term in kalam.
2
Understand why al-Nazzam needed to extend it
Al-Nazzam took a proof that originally applied to accidents and argued that it also applies to bodies. Ask: what is his basis for this extension?
Refer to the previous lesson for his view on body composition.
3
Reflect on al-Amidi's comment
Al-Amidi notes that the extension is built on al-Nazzam's principle that the substance is composed of accidents. This means the extension is not automatic but requires justification.
Think about why al-Amidi says this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Therefore, the need for extending the proof is the same on both views (body as aggregate of accidents vs. body as composed of atoms) because the basis for negating the need is that a composite of mere accidents is itself an accident by intuition. So the proof of accident renewal automatically includes the body, and extension is unnecessary. The passage concludes that the lack of need is based on intuitive reason, not on al-Nazzam's claim of substantiality. It leaves open the possibility of another transmitted view (the part) but says the author did not mention it. The argument shows the theological subtlety of how different views of body composition affect the application of proofs.

Key Terms

الآمدي
الجوهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Extending the proof means applying the same conclusion to unrelated things.
Correction: Extending the proof means applying it to the particulars of the same category when there is obscurity.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of 'sard al-dalil' according to the passage?
A) Establishing a new proof for bodies
B) Applying the proof to its particulars due to obscurity
C) Denying the proof for accidents
D) Repeating the proof for emphasis

Key Takeaway
Sard al-dalil is the application of a proof to its particulars when there is obscurity; al-Nazzam extended the proof of accident renewal to bodies based on his principle that substance is composed of accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Nazzam extended a proof based on a principle he held without question. What principles do you rely on when applying Islamic teachings to modern issues? Have you ever examined whether those principles are truly from revelation or just inherited assumptions?
?
If the underlying principle is wrong, the whole extension collapses. Think of a belief you hold that you have never questioned. What would it take for you to re-examine it? Are you willing to test your foundations, or do you fear what you might find?
?
Al-Amidi pointed out that the necessity of renewal does not negate the need for extending the proof. Do you ever assume that something is automatically true without properly extending the evidence? When has that led you to a mistaken conclusion in your faith or life?

Individual Atoms and the Need for Proof Extension
Argues that al-Nazzam's atom view does not affect the need for proof extension, and concludes that the lack of need is based on intuition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Nazzam's view on individual atoms (jawahir afrad) does not change the need to extend the proof, and the role of intuition in establishing the body's accidental nature.

Core Idea
Even if body is composed of atoms, each atom is still made of accidents, so body is accident by intuition.

This lesson tackles a discussion about whether the need to extend the proof changes if the body is composed of individual atoms (jawahir afrad). The commentator argues that for al-Nazzam, even the individual atom is a composite of accidents, so the body is inevitably an accident by intuition. Thus, the proof for accident renewal automatically covers the body, and no extension is needed. The lesson also touches on a possible alternate transmission.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two views
The passage considers both the view that body is an aggregate of accidents and the view that body is composed of individual atoms. Locate these two descriptions.
These are the two positions attributed to al-Nazzam.
2
Understand why they lead to the same conclusion
Even if the body is atoms, each atom is itself a composite of accidents. So a composite of mere accidents is necessarily an accident by intuition (badiha). Thus the proof for accidents includes the body.
This is the key argument.
3
Reflect on the role of intuition (badiha)
The passage emphasises that it is intuitive reason, not al-Nazzam's specific doctrine, that justifies the lack of need for extension. Ask yourself: What role does intuition play?
This anchors the argument in common reason.
4
Consider the exception
The passage mentions a possible alternate transmission where al-Nazzam might have held a different view about the part (al-juz'). Think about how this would affect the argument.
The commentator sets this aside as not mentioned by the author.

His statement: (It follows from the renewal of accidents the renewal of bodies) because what is understood from his statement that the body is a collection of accidents is that they (the accidents) remain upon their accidentally and by aggregation become bodies, so their renewal necessitates their renewal. However, in Sharh al-Tajrid it is mentioned that what is recorded in the books of the Mu'tazila is that things like modes (akwan), beliefs, pains, pleasures, and the like are accidents that have no entry in the reality of the body by agreement. As for colors, tastes, smells, sounds, and tangible qualities like heat and cold and others, according to al-Nazzam they are substances (jawahir) rather bodies (ajsam), for he explicitly stated that each of these is a subtle thing composed of aggregated substances, and then those subtle bodies when they aggregate and interpenetrate become the dense body which is the inanimate. His statement: (without need to extend the proof (sard al-dalil)) perhaps the meaning of 'extending the proof' is applying it to the particulars of the things for which the proof was established, due to obscurity therein, as will come in the notice mentioned in the third objective: bodies are enduring. For he said: The proof when it was established for accidents, al-Nazzam extended it to bodies and said they also do not endure. Al-Amidi said: That is because it is built upon his principle – that the substance (jawhar) is composed of accidents. End of quote. On this basis, the necessity of bodies' renewal from the renewal of accidents does not negate the need for extending the proof. His statement: (And it is only needed if bodies in his view are composed of individual atoms (jawahir afrad).) There is discussion in it, because composition of the body from infinite individual atoms – though it is well-known from his doctrine – nevertheless the individual atom, nay substance absolutely, is in his view composed of mere aggregated accidents, as will come in the station of substance. So the infinite parts are in his view infinite substances each composed of aggregated accidents. There is no difference between the two views concerning the aforementioned need or lack thereof, because the basis for negating the need on the assumption that the body is a collection of aggregated accidents is that a composite of mere accidents necessarily is an accident, as intuition (badiha) testifies, even if it is a substance according to al-Nazzam. So the proof indicating the renewal of accidents indicates the renewal of the body because it falls under them, so there is no need to extend the proof to it, just as there is no need to extend it to specifics of accidents. And this implication does not differ between the two views, as is not hidden from the contemplator. Unless there is established from him another transmitted statement – namely the saying of the part (al-juz') in the manner spoken by the rest of the theologians, even if the author did not mention it. And by what we have mentioned it becomes apparent that the lack of need for extending the proof is according to what intuition testifies, not according to what al-Nazzam claimed of the substantiality of his doctrine.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a lemma from al-Mawaqif regarding the implication of accident renewal for body renewal under al-Nazzam's doctrine. It begins by explaining the lemma: if the body is a collection of accidents (as al-Nazzam held), then the renewal of accidents necessarily entails the renewal of the body, because the body is nothing but those accidents. However, it notes an alternative from Sharh al-Tajrid: the Mu'tazila agreed that certain things (e.g., modes, beliefs, pains) are accidents not constitutive of the body, but colors, tastes, etc. were considered by al-Nazzam to be subtle bodies that aggregate to form dense bodies. The passage then discusses the phrase 'without need to extend the proof' (sard al-dalil). It explains that extending the proof means applying it to the particulars of the things for which the proof was originally established (here, accidents) due to some obscurity. It recounts that al-Nazzam extended the proof from accidents to bodies, claiming bodies also do not endure. Al-Amidi says this is because al-Nazzam's principle is that the substance (jawhar) is composed of accidents. So the renewal of accidents does not by itself negate the need to extend the proof. The passage then examines the condition 'if bodies are composed of individual atoms (jawahir afrad)'. Despite this being famed as al-Nazzam's view, the commentator argues that for al-Nazzam even the individual atom is composed of mere aggregated accidents (as will be explained later). Therefore, the need for extending the proof is the same on both views (body as aggregate of accidents vs. body as composed of atoms) because the basis for negating the need is that a composite of mere accidents is itself an accident by intuition. So the proof of accident renewal automatically includes the body, and extension is unnecessary. The passage concludes that the lack of need is based on intuitive reason, not on al-Nazzam's claim of substantiality. It leaves open the possibility of another transmitted view (the part) but says the author did not mention it. The argument shows the theological subtlety of how different views of body composition affect the application of proofs.
