License and the Pleonastic 'Ka'
Critique of alternative interpretation and explanation of 'ka' as pleonastic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's critique of a forced alternative interpretation and the rationale for using 'ka' (like) in the phrase 'like number' as pleonastic.

Core Idea
The 'ka' in 'like number' is pleonastic, not an indication of plurality.

This passage dives into textual minutiae. The commentator first dismantles a convoluted grammatical interpretation that tries to avoid the notion of license. Then, he clarifies the occurrence of 'ka' (like) in the example 'like number,' showing it is pleonastic and does not undermine the confinement of discrete quantity to number. This reveals how examples are expressed and defended.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of license
The passage begins with commentary on the phrase 'license in expression.' The commentator rejects a forced justification that tries to avoid license in the example.
Note the sharp criticism: the alternative interpretation is called 'extremely foolish.'
2
Examine the critique of the alternative interpretation
The alternative involves omitting the pronoun of the matter and supplying an adverbial phrase, which is grammatically far-fetched.
3
Analyze the use of 'ka' in 'like number'
The 'ka' is introduced because it is not self-evident that discrete quantity is confined to number; a proof is needed. At first glance, discrete might be other things.
4
Recognize the pleonastic nature of 'ka'
The commentator states that 'ka' is pleonastic, as in other expressions, so it does not contradict the confinement of discrete to number.
This resolves the apparent tension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (تجوز في العبارة) حيث أطلق الجزء وأراد النقطة الحالة في الخط بجامع أن كلاً منهما مفروض في الخط مثلا وما قيل في توجيهه بأن ضمير الشأن من أن محذوف وهو اسمه وأي جزء ظرف والضمير في فرض للحد والمعنى، فإن الشأن أنَّ الحد في أي جزء فرض يكون بداية ونهاية فلا مسامحة، ففي غاية السخافة لأنه يلزم حذف ضمير الشأن منصوباً وتقدير كلمة في في ظرف المكان الغير المبهم وإرجاع ضمير فرض إلى ما هو بعيد عن الفهم من تقدير لفظ أنَّ الحد كما قدره ليتم المعنى . قوله : (كالعدد) أورد الكاف لأن انحصاره في العدد محتاج إلى دليل كما سيجيء ففي بادئ الرأي يحتمل غيره . قوله : ( فإنك إن أشرت إلخ) الظاهر أن يقول : لأن كل واحد من الوحدات التي هي أجزاء العدد ليس لها حدود فضلا عن اشتراكها، ولعله راعى في ذلك البيان بتركب كل مرتبة منه مما تحته كما هو السابق إلى الوهم . قوله : فالمنفصل (كالعدد الكاف مقحمة كما في قولهم : الخفيف المطلق كالنار على ما أشار إليه الشارح في حواشي بيان المفتاح فلا ينافي إيراد الكاف انحصار المنفصل في العدد كما صرح به وقد يجعل إيراد الكاف في مثله باعتبار الأفراد الذهنية
His statement: '(License in expression)' – where he used 'part' generally and intended the point which is a state in the line, due to the common factor that each is assumed in the line, for example. And what was said in its justification, that the pronoun of the matter from 'an' is omitted, and it is its noun, and 'ayy juz'' is an adverb, and the pronoun in 'furiḍa' refers to the boundary, not the part, and the meaning: 'the matter is that the boundary, in any part assumed, is a beginning and an end', so there is no license – this is extremely foolish because it necessitates omitting the pronoun of the matter in the accusative and supplying the word 'fī' (in) in the adverbial place of non-vague space, and referring the pronoun of 'furiḍa' to something far from understanding by estimating the phrase 'that the boundary' as he estimated to complete the meaning. His statement: '(Like number)' – he adduced the 'ka' (like) because its confinement to number needs a proof, as will come, so at first glance it could be other. His statement: ('For if you point, etc.') – apparently he should say: 'because each one of the units that are parts of number has no boundaries, let alone sharing them', and perhaps he observed in that the explanation by the composition of each rank from what is below it, as is the first thing that occurs to the mind. His statement: 'Then the discrete (like number' – the 'ka' is pleonastic, as in their saying 'the absolute light is like fire', as the commentator indicated in the annotations of Bayān al-Miftāḥ, so the mention of 'ka' does not contradict the confinement of the discrete to number, as he stated, and the mention of 'ka' in such cases may be considered with respect to mental individuals.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on specific phrases. First, it criticizes the alternative grammatical interpretation that avoids the license, calling it extremely foolish because it involves unlikely grammatical suppositions. Then it discusses the phrase 'like number' (ka al-ʿadad): the 'ka' (like) is used because it is not obvious that discrete quantity is confined to number, so a proof is needed. The commentator then suggests that the example could have been phrased differently, but the author may have intended a certain explanation. Finally, it states that the 'ka' in such expressions can be pleonastic, as in other examples, and thus does not negate the confinement of discrete to number.

Refutation of a Grammatical Justification for Point as Part
The commentator refutes a grammatical justification for calling a point a part of a line.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator rejects a proposed grammatical interpretation that tries to make the point a literal part of the line, and why such an interpretation is considered extremely absurd.

Core Idea
Grammatical tricks cannot turn a figurative expression literal.

In this passage, the commentator engages with a grammatical attempt to justify the author's statement that calling a point a part of the line is permissible in expression. He first restates the author's figurative use, then exposes the absurdity of a proposed literalistic interpretation. This lesson unpacks the commentator's reasoning and shows why excessive grammatical reconstruction fails.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The author said 'permissible in expression' — meaning he used 'part' figuratively for the point. Recognize that this is not a literal claim.
The commentator clarifies that the author intended the point situated in the line.
2
Read the proposed interpretation
The proposed interpretation tries to make it literal by assuming deleted grammatical elements and treating 'part' as an adverb of place.
It claims there is no license (musāmaha) in the expression.
3
Identify the three grammatical problems
The commentator lists three requirements that make the interpretation absurd: deleting the pronoun of the matter in accusative, inserting 'fī' into a non-vague adverb, and referring a pronoun to an estimated phrase.
Each problem alone would be questionable; combined they show the interpretation is forced.
4
Appreciate the conclusion
The commentator dismisses the interpretation as 'extremely absurd' (fī ghāyat al-sakhāfa), upholding the figurative nature of the expression.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (تجوز في العبارة) حيث أطلق الجزء وأراد النقطة الحالة في الخط بجامع أن كلاً منهما مفروض في الخط مثلا وما قيل في توجيهه بأن ضمير الشأن من أن محذوف وهو اسمه وأي جزء ظرف والضمير في فرض للحد والمعنى، فإن الشأن أنَّ الحد في أي جزء فرض يكون بداية ونهاية فلا مسامحة، ففي غاية السخافة لأنه يلزم حذف ضمير الشأن منصوباً وتقدير كلمة في في ظرف المكان الغير المبهم وإرجاع ضمير فرض إلى ما هو بعيد عن الفهم من تقدير لفظ أنَّ الحد كما قدره ليتم المعنى .
His statement: '(permissible in expression) because he used 'part' intending the point situated in the line, by virtue that each of them is assumed in the line, for example. And what was said in its interpretation—that the pronoun of the matter (ḍamīr al-sha'n) from 'an is deleted and it is its subject, and 'ayyu juz'in is an adverb, and the pronoun in 'furīḍa' is for the boundary (ḥadd) and the meaning: the matter is that the boundary in whatever part it is assumed is a beginning and an end, so there is no license—is extremely absurd, because it requires deleting the pronoun of the matter in the accusative case, and estimating the word 'fī' in an adverb of place that is not vague, and referring the pronoun of 'furīḍa' to what is far from understanding, by estimating the phrase 'anna al-ḥadd' as he estimated to complete the meaning.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on a linguistic attempt to justify the author's statement that calling the point a part of the line is 'permissible in expression'. The commentator first clarifies that the author used the word 'part' figuratively, intending the point that lies on the line, because both the point and the part are assumed to exist on the line. Then he mentions a proposed grammatical interpretation: that there is an implicit pronoun of the matter (ḍamīr al-sha'n) in the phrase 'an' (that), making it the subject of a nominal sentence; that 'ayyu juz'in (whichever part) is an adverb of place; and that the pronoun in 'furīḍa' (is assumed) refers to the boundary (ḥadd), meaning that the boundary in whichever part it is assumed is both a beginning and an end, so there is no concession (musāmaha). The commentator dismisses this interpretation as utterly absurd (fī ghāyat al-sakhāfa) because it would require: (1) deleting the pronoun of the matter in the accusative case, (2) inserting the word 'fī' into an adverb of place that is not vague, and (3) making the pronoun of 'furīḍa' refer to something that is far from obvious, namely the estimated phrase 'anna al-ḥadd' (that the boundary) as he estimated, in order to complete the meaning. Thus, the commentator upholds the figurative nature of the expression and rejects any literalistic grammatical defense.

Key Terms
تجوز في العبارة
ضمير الشأن
حد
مسامحة
سخافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the point is literally a part of the line in the same way a segment is.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that it is figurative ('permissible in expression'), and any grammatical attempt to make it literal is rejected as absurd.

Checkpoint

A) Plausible and sound
B) Extremely absurd
C) Moderately acceptable
D) Requires further study

Key Takeaway
Any grammatical interpretation that forces a figurative expression into a literal one, especially by requiring multiple unjustified deletions and assumptions, is absurd and should be rejected.

Why 'Like Number'? The Function of 'Ka' in a Philosophical Example
The commentator explains the author's use of 'ka' in 'like number'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author deliberately used the particle 'ka' (like) in the phrase 'like number' and how it signals the need for proof.

Core Idea
The particle 'ka' signals that a claim requires proof, not assumption.

In this short passage, the commentator glosses the author's choice of wording. He points out that the author did not simply say 'number' but 'like number', because the idea that discontinuous quantity is only number is not self-evident. The particle 'ka' is used to indicate that this confinement requires demonstration, which will be provided later.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic sentence and its English translation. Notice the use of 'ka' at the beginning.
2
Identify the key term: 'ka'
The particle 'ka' means 'like'. Understand that it is used here to express similarity or approximation, not certainty.
3
Understand the author's caution
The commentator explains that 'ka' is used because the claim that discontinuous quantity is exclusively number is not obvious; it needs proof.
The proof is promised later in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( كالعدد) أورد الكاف لأن انحصاره في العدد محتاج إلى دليل كما سيجيء ففي بادئ الرأي يحتمل غيره .
His statement: '(like number) He used the 'ka' because its being confined to number requires proof, as will come; so at first glance, it might be something else.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This short passage is a gloss on the author's expression 'like number' (ka-l-‘adad). The commentator notes that the author deliberately used the particle 'ka' (meaning 'like') because the claim that discontinuous quantity is exclusively number (inḥiṣāruhu fī al-‘adad) is not self-evident; it requires proof (muḥtāj ilā dalīl), which will be provided later in the text. At first glance (fī bādi’ al-ra’y), one might think that discontinuous quantity could be something other than number, hence the cautious phrasing with 'ka'. This explains the author's choice of wording and prepares the reader for the forthcoming demonstration.

Key Takeaway

Essential and Accidental Quantity in Discrete Quantity
Distinguishes essential and accidental quantity in discrete quantity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how discrete quantity can be considered as quantity by essence or by accident, and why only the former is a genuine category.

Core Idea
Discrete quantity is essential when units are taken merely as units; accidental when taken as specific things.

This passage from the commentary on al-Mawaqif clarifies a subtle but crucial distinction in the philosophy of quantity. When we count things, the resulting number can be considered in two ways: as a collection of mere units (essential quantity) or as a collection of specific objects that happen to be units (accidental quantity). The commentator explains this distinction and why only essential quantity counts as a true category.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation to get the overall meaning.
Pay attention to the terms āḥād (ones) and wāḥidāt (units).
2
Identify the two ways of taking units
The passage presents two ways: taking the one merely as one, or taking it as a specific thing. Notice how each leads to a different kind of quantity.
3
Understand essential vs. accidental quantity
Essential quantity (kamm bi-dh-dhāt) arises from the first way; accidental quantity (kamm bi-l-ʿaraḍ) from the second. The commentator explains why the first is a category.
The discussion focuses on essential quantity because it is one of the ten categories.
4
Analyze the objection and answer
A potential objection is raised: units taken as mere ones are indeterminate, not units. The answer clarifies that the unity is self-identical, so they are indeed units.
This shows the depth of the analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمفردات آحاد والواحد إما أن يؤخذ من حيث هو واحد من غير أن يلاحظ معه شيء آخر أو يؤخذ من حيث إنه واحد هو شيء معين فالآحاد المأخوذة على الوجه الأول وحدات مجتمعات بينهما انفصال ذاتي فيكون عدداً مبلغه تلك الوحدات فهي كم بالذات والمأخوذة على الوجه الثاني أمور معروضة للوحدات منفصلة بانفصال الوحدات فهي كم بالعرض وإلى هذا المعنى أشار بقوله ( لأنه ) أي الكم المنفصل أن ر لا بد ينتهي إلى وحدات ( أي إلى آحاد كما عرفت ) والوحدة إن كانت نفس ذاتها ) أي نفس ذات تلك الآحاد بأن تكون مأخوذة من حيث إنها آحاد فقط ( فهو ) أي المجتمع من تلك الآحاد ( الكثرة ) التي هي العدد ( وإن ( كانت الوحدة ( عارضة لها أي لتلك الآحاد بأن تكون مأخوذة من حيث إنها أشياء معينة موصوفة بالوحدات ) فهي كم بالعرض والكلام في الكم بالذات )؛ لأنه الذي عد مقولة من المقولات . قوله : ( أي إلى آحاد فسر الوحدات بالآحاد ليصح الترديد المذكور بقوله : إن كانت إلخ . قوله : ( وهو الامتداد إلخ) المقصود من تفسير الألفاظ الثلاثة هاهنا بيان أنها تطلق على الأبعاد الثلاثة بهذه المعاني والمقصود مما سيجيء في المتن أن هذه الألفاظ تطلق على هذه المعاني فلا تكرار . قوله : ( فإنه إلخ ) تعليل لتعميم المرجع وحمله على خلاف الظاهر. عدداً وبأن القول كم منفصل بلا واسطة غير قار الذات، كما أن العدد كم منفصل قار الذات ووجه الدفع ظاهر من الاستدلال الذي ذكره فليتأمل. قوله : ( فالآحاد المذكورة على الوجه الأول وحدات فإن قلت : الآحاد المأخوذة على الوجه الأول آحاد غير معينة لا وحدات . قلت : لا منافاة لأن الوحدة واحد بوحدة هي نفسه على ما تقرر فالوحدات آحاد ووحدات .
And the individual units are ones, and the one is either taken insofar as it is one without considering anything else with it, or it is taken insofar as it is one as a specific thing. So the ones taken in the first way are units collected together with a separation of essence between them, so they become a number whose amount is those units, so they are quantity by essence. And those taken in the second way are things that are accidents to the units, separated by the separation of the units, so they are quantity by accident. And to this meaning he indicated by his saying (because) i.e. the discrete quantity that inevitably ends in units (i.e. in ones as you have known). And if the unit is the essence itself (i.e. the essence itself of those ones, by being taken insofar as they are only ones), then it (i.e. the aggregate of those ones) is the plurality which is number. And if the unit is accidental to them (i.e. to those ones, by being taken insofar as they are specific things described by units), then it is quantity by accident. And the discourse is about quantity by essence because it is counted among the categories. His saying: (i.e. to ones) he explained units as ones to make the mentioned disjunction correct by his saying: if it is etc. His saying: (and it is the extension etc.) the purpose of the interpretation of the three terms here is to state that they are applied to the three dimensions with these meanings, and the purpose of what will come in the text is that these terms are applied to these meanings, so no repetition. His saying: (for it etc.) a reason for generalizing the referent and taking it contrary to the apparent. Number and that the discrete quantity without mediation is non-stationary essence, just as number is discrete quantity stationary essence, and the way of refutation is clear from the argument he mentioned, so let it be considered. His saying: (then the ones mentioned in the first way are units. For if you say: the ones taken in the first way are indeterminate ones, not units. I say: there is no contradiction, because the unit is one by a unity which is itself, as established, so the units are ones and units.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a distinction between two ways of considering units in discrete quantity (al-kamm al-munfasil). The commentator explains that when we take individuals (āḥād) merely as units without any additional determination, they form a number (ʿadad) by essential separation, and this is quantity by essence (kamm bi-dh-dhāt). When we take them as specific things that have the property of being units, the number arises accidentally, and this is quantity by accident (kamm bi-l-ʿaraḍ). The commentator then clarifies the author's statements, emphasizing that the discussion is focused on essential quantity because it is a genuine category (maqūla). He also addresses a potential objection about units being indeterminate and resolves it by noting that the unity is self-identical.

الكم بالعرض
الوحدات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any collection of units automatically yields quantity by essence.
Correction: It depends on how the units are taken: as mere units (essential) or as specific things described by units (accidental).

Checkpoint
What is the difference between quantity by essence (kamm bi-dh-dhāt) and quantity by accident (kamm bi-l-ʿaraḍ) in discrete quantity?
A) Quantity by essence is real while quantity by accident is imaginary.
B) Quantity by essence arises when units are taken merely as units, while quantity by accident arises when units are taken as specific things with the property of being units.
C) Quantity by essence is continuous while quantity by accident is discrete.
D) Quantity by essence is a category of substance while quantity by accident is a category of quality.

Key Takeaway
Essential quantity is a genuine category because it arises from units considered merely as units, while accidental quantity depends on the specific things those units represent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold that you treat as essential because it is associated with a number (e.g., 'I pray five times a day'). Do you focus on the mere fact that it is a number of actions (essential quantity), or do you focus on the specific actions themselves (accidental quantity)? How might this distinction affect the sincerity of your worship? Commit to examining one such belief this week.
?
In your study of Islamic theology, have you ever assumed that a certain concept belongs to the category of substance or quality when it might actually be a quantity? For example, do you treat 'forgiveness' as something that comes in degrees (quantity) or as a quality? Let this lesson remind you to be precise about categories. Write down one area where you might have confused essence and accident in your understanding of a religious matter, and resolve to clarify it.
?
The passage shows that units taken merely as units are true units, even though they are indeterminate. When you reflect on the oneness of Allah, do you sometimes confuse the numerical oneness (essential) with the specific attributes (accidental)? How can you ensure that your tawhid is based on the essence and not on accidental qualities? Pause and make a conscious intention today to purify your belief.

The Three Dimensions Defined
Defines thickness and the primary meanings of the three dimensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the foundational definitions of the three dimensions — length, width, and depth — as pure quantities, and how thickness relates to the mathematical body.

Core Idea
Thickness is the filling between surfaces, and each dimension is a primary quantity.

This lesson introduces the core definitions of the three dimensions as they appear in classical kalam. The author begins by explaining thickness (thikhn) as the filling between surfaces, identifying it with the mathematical body. Then he clarifies that length, width, and depth can be considered pure quantities: length as a line, width as a surface, and depth as thickness itself. Understanding these baseline meanings is essential before exploring their additional applications.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of thickness
Examine the Arabic and translation for the definition of thickness (thikhn) as 'the filling between surfaces' and its connection to the mathematical body.
The commentary clarifies that 'filling' means the filled thing, not the process.
2
Understand 'deep' as a property of body
Note the statement: 'if a body, then it is in itself deep.' Reflect on what this means for the nature of bodies.
3
Identify the three pure quantities
Find where the text says length is the line, width is the surface, and depth is thickness. See how these serve as the simplest meanings.
These are called 'pure quantities' because they have no additional relation.

Second observation - Third purpose: The three dimensions of the body. It is said for thickness: it is the filling between surfaces, meaning the mathematical body which is enclosed by one surface or two surfaces or surfaces without additional condition, and in this sense it is said: if a body, then it is in itself deep. And it is said for descending thickness, i.e., thickness qualified by descent, and it is then called ascending thickness, i.e., qualified by ascent, thickness, and in this regard it is said: depth of the well and thickness of the minaret. And it is said: length and width and depth have other meanings besides what was mentioned, such as it is said: length is the extension extending from the center of the world to its circumference, which is the fourth of the meanings of length. And it is said: length also is the extension extending from a human's head to his feet and from the head of quadrupeds to their rear, and this is its meaning. And it is said: width is the extension from a human's right or quadrupeds' right to their left, which is the fourth of the meanings of width. And it is said: depth is the extension from a human's chest to his back and from the back of quadrupeds to the ground, and this is the fourth of the meanings of depth. And know that these mentioned meanings of length, width, and depth are some pure quantities, like length in the sense of the single extension which is the line, width in the sense of the surface, and depth in the sense of thickness. His saying: 'It is the filling between surfaces' thus in a place in the Shifa and in another place it is said depth for the thickness that the surfaces enclose, the intended meaning of 'filling' is that with which it is filled, and the addition of 'filling' to 'what' is lamiyya as is apparent, i.e., filling of an imagined void between surfaces. And it is said that the word 'what' is indefinite and relative, and the addition of 'filling' to it is explicative, thus directing 'filling'. His saying: 'which is enclosed by one surface' like the solid sphere, he indicated by this that mentioning surface as an example, since the intention is not to give a comprehensive and exclusive definition but only a general application to the meanings. His saying: 'and it is said length etc.' he did not combine them with the previous meanings, indicating the abundance of the first applications. His saying: 'from the center of the world to its circumference' like the length of a human, which is the dimension in which the first movement of growth occurs, thus in the Shifa. His saying: 'to the ground' i.e., to its lower part which is on the side of the ground. His saying: 'the single extension which is the line' he states here that the first meaning is the line itself, and it indicates. His saying: 'filling between surfaces meaning the mathematical body etc.' The expression of the text, since it apparently does not include the mathematical body that is in the solid sphere, for it has one surface not multiple surfaces, and includes the natural body, the commentator interpreted it explicitly as intended, although his taking it from the author's phrase is strained. And if he had replaced 'meaning' with 'i.e.', it would be clearer. Then what is in his saying 'filling between surfaces' is either an intensified indefinite or a relative, and the addition of 'filling' to it is explicative. In both cases the intended meaning of 'filling' is not the verbal noun nor the product as thought, but rather the filled thing either terminologically or as a linguistic metaphor. [PAGE_068] Second observation - Third purpose: The three dimensions of the body. C which is the mathematical body. And among them are quantities taken with addition to another matter, like the second assumed or first or third: for the extension being assumed second is an addition to the first assumed and vice versa, and being assumed third is an addition to the sum of the first two; just as the sum also has an addition to it. And sometimes a third addition is considered with it (i.e., with the quantity), like 'longer' (al-atwal): for it is longer in relation to what is long measured against a short one, so here there are two additions: the longer-ness and the length that is relative to shortness. But he expressed the longer-ness as the third addition because it is accidental to a third matter after two matters between which length is realized, or because he considered that shortness is also an addition opposite to length. But there is distance because shortness is not taken with the longer. And if he had expressed it as the second addition, it would be clearer. Or a fourth addition, like 'longer' in relation to another 'longer' in relation to yet another, so there are three additions: two longer-nesses and one relative length. The first longer-ness is accidental to a fourth matter, so he made it a fourth addition by analogy to what preceded, but making it third is preferable. And in the Eastern Investigations, these quantities when taken as added to something, they are sometimes taken such that it is not a condition of their addition to that thing that they be added to something else. And sometimes they are taken such that it is a condition of their addition to something that they be added to a third thing. Example of the first: it is said this line is long when it is said: upon it his saying: i.e., in itself dimension and single extension. So the apparent is that he should say: every line is in itself length, as occurred in the Tajrid: the line is length without width. Perhaps he intended by 'long' (tawil) length (tul), because it is qualified by length in the sense of 'having length' even if it is the same as 'length' in the sense of 'extension'. And this strained interpretation, even if it validates applying 'long' to it, does not validate his saying 'and in this sense etc.' because in this sense it is said: every line is length, and it does not apply in his saying: every surface is wide and every body is deep. His saying: 'and in the Eastern Investigations etc.' supports what he mentioned previously: that in 'longer' in relation to 'long' there are two additions, and in relation to other than 'longer' there are three additions. His saying: 'just as for their sum also there is an addition to it' realizes the first and second, although it does not necessitate realizing the third nor conceiving it, except that the discourse is about bodily dimensions, so for the first two there is an addition to the third, for length and width are determined by depth. His saying: 'and if he had expressed it as the second addition it would be clearer' then if you say: the 'longer' that the author mentioned is from length in the sense of the longer of two extensions, so it has two increases and length is also an addition, so it is correct to say that in 'longer' a third addition is considered without strain. I say: the mentioned length is in the sense of extension absolutely and is not an addition. The closest in directing the author's words is to say: 'longer' is he whose length increased over another's length in relation to a short one, so in it there are two relative lengths and a relative increase, so there is no flaw in the speech.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the three dimensions (length, width, depth) in multiple senses, followed by commentary. The text first defines thickness (thikhn) as the filling between surfaces, i.e., the mathematical body enclosed by one or more surfaces. It notes that in this sense, any body is deep in itself. It then distinguishes between descending thickness (depth of a well) and ascending thickness (thickness of a minaret). Next, it lists four meanings for each dimension: length as (1) the single extension (line), (2) from center of world to circumferences, (3) from head to foot of humans or animals, (4) the extension from right to left? Actually the fourth meaning of length is from center of world to circumference. The text then clarifies that some of these meanings are pure quantities (e.g., length as line, width as surface, depth as thickness) while others involve addition (e.g., specific orientations). The commentary on 'filling between surfaces' explains that 'filling' (hashw) refers to what fills, and the phrase is interpreted as either indefinite or relative, with an explicative addition. It notes that the example of a single surface (solid sphere) is illustrative, not a strict definition. The commentary also discusses the mathematical vs. natural body and the strained interpretation of the author's phrase. On page 68, the commentary continues with how these quantities are taken with addition, e.g., the 'longer' (atwal) involves two or three additions depending on relativity. It cites the Eastern Investigations on conditionality of addition. The discussion ends with clarification that 'longer' means one whose length exceeds another relative to a short one, involving two relative lengths and a relative increase.

Key Terms
ثخن (thikhn - thickness)
حشو (hashw - filling)
جسم تعليمي (mathematical body)
طول (tul - length)
عرض (ard - width)
عمق (umq - depth)
كمية (quantity)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'filling' (hashw) means the act of filling.
Correction: Commentary clarifies it means the filled thing, either terminologically or as linguistic metaphor.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the meaning of 'thickness' (thikhn) as a pure quantity?
A) The extension from head to foot
B) The filling between surfaces
C) The distance from center to circumference
D) The surface area of a body

Key Takeaway
Thickness is the fundamental filling that constitutes the mathematical body, and the three dimensions have pure quantitative meanings: line, surface, and thickness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own hand, do you see only its surface length and width? Or do you remember that it has a thickness—a filling that makes it a real body? How does recognizing this depth change your sense of the realness of Allah's creation?
?
The text teaches that length, width, and depth have pure meanings independent of your body. How often do you view the world around you from this absolute perspective? What would it take for you to start seeing creation as it is in itself, rather than always centered on yourself?
?
You learned that any body, by being a body, is 'deep in itself.' This interior depth is unseen by you but fully known by Allah. How does this truth confront your tendency to judge things only by their surface? Will you today ask Allah to give you insight into the unseen realities beneath visible forms?

Four Meanings of Each Dimension
Lists additional meanings for length, width, and depth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four distinct meanings of length, width, and depth, including their astronomical and anatomical applications.

Core Idea
Each dimension has multiple senses beyond its pure quantity definition.

After establishing the pure quantities, the author lists additional meanings for each dimension. For length, there is the astronomical sense (from world's center to circumference) and the anatomical sense (from head to foot). Width includes right-to-left extension, and depth includes chest-to-back orientation. These help apply dimensional concepts to concrete objects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four meanings of length
The text gives three specific meanings: line, from center to circumference, and from head to foot. Find these and note the commentary's mention of a fourth.
The fourth meaning is perhaps the more general concept of length as any extension.
2
Contrast width and depth meanings
Width is given as right-to-left extension (human or quadruped), and depth as chest-to-back (human) or back-to-ground (quadruped).
3
Understand the list structure
The author calls some of these the 'fourth' meaning, implying a prior classification.
The commentary indicates that the first three meanings were discussed elsewhere.

قوله : ( وهو حشو ما بين السطوح هكذا في موضع من الشفاء وفي موضع آخر ويقل عمق للشخن الذي يحصره السطوح فالمراد بالحشو ما يحشى به وإضافة الحشو إلى ما لامية كما هو الظاهر أي حشو خلاء متوهم بين السطوح، وما قيل : إن كلمة ما إبهامية وموصولة وإضافة الحشو إليه بيانية فتوجيه حشو . قوله : (الذي يحصره سطح واحد كالكرة المصمتة أشار به إلى أن ذكر السطح بطريق التمثيل إذ ليس المقصود بيان حد جامع مانع بل مجرد الإطلاق على المعاني . قوله : ( ويقل الطول إلخ ) لم يجمعها مع المعاني السابقة إشارة إلى كثرة الإطلاقات الأول. قوله : (من مركز العالم إلى محيطه كطول الإنسان وهو البعد الذي فيه أول حركة النشو كذا في الشفاء .
قوله : ( إلى الأرض أي إلى أسفله الذي في جانب الأرض . قوله : ( الامتداد الواحد الذي هو الخط صرح هاهنا بأن المعنى الأول هو نفس الخط ويدل
قوله : ( حشو ما بين السطوح أعني الجسم التعليمي إلخ) عبارة المتن لما لم يكن بحسب الظاهر متناولاً للجسم التعليمي الذي في الكرة المصمتة فإن فيها سطحاً واحداً لا سطوحاً ومتناولاً للجسم الطبيعي فسرها الشارح تصريحاً بالمقصود وإن كان أخذه من عبارة المصنف تعسفاً ولو بدل أعني بيعني لكان أظهر ثم إنَّ ما في قوله حشو ما بين السطوح إما مشددة إبهامية أو موصولة وإضافة الحشو إليه بيانية، وعلى التقديرين ليس المراد بالحشو المعنى المصدري ولا الحاصل به كما ظن ، بل المحشوبة إما اصطلاحاً أو المجاز اللغوي.

المرصد الثاني - المقصد الثالث : الأبعاد الثلاثة للجسم
C
الذي هو الجسم التعليمي ( ومنها ما هي كميات ) مأخوذة ( مع إضافة ) إلى أمر آخر ( كالمفروض ثانياً ) أو أولاً أو ثالثاً فإن كون الامتداد مفروضاً ثانياً إضافة له إلى المفروض أولاً وبالعكس وكونه مفروضاً ثالثاً إضافة إلى مجموع الأولين؛ كما أنَّ لمجموعهما أيضاً إضافة إليه ) وقد يعتبر معه ) أي مع الكم (إضافة ثالثة كالأطول ) فإنه أطول بالقياس إلى ما هو طويل مقيساً إلى قصير فهاهنا إضافتان الأطولية والطول المضايف للقصر لكنه عبر عن الأطولية بالإضافة الثالثة لأنها عارضة الأمر ثالث بعد أمرين تحقق بينهما الطول، أو لأنه نظر إلى أن القصر أيضاً إضافة مقابلة للطول وفيه بعد لأن القصر ليس مأخوذاً مع الأطول، ولو عبر عنها بالإضافة الثانية، لكان أظهر ( أو ) إضافة ( رابعة كالأطول بالنسبة إلى الغير الأطول بالقياس إلى آخر فيكون هناك ثلاث إضافات أطوليتان وطول إضافي . والأطولية الأولى عارضة لأمر رابع فجعلها إضافة رابعة على قياس ما مر، وجعلها ثالثة أولى وفي المباحث المشرقية أن هذه الكميات إذا أخذت مضافة إلى شيء فقد تؤخذ تارة بحيث لا يكون من شرط إضافتها إلى ذلك الشيء إضافتها إلى شيء آخر. وقد تؤخذ تارة أخرى بحيث يكون من شرط إضافتها إلى شيء إضافتها إلى شيء ثالث . مثال الأول أن يقال : هذا الخط طويل عندما يقال :
عليه قوله أي في نفسه بعد وامتداد واحد ، فالظاهر أن يقول : إن كل خط في نفسه طول كما وقع في التجريد الخط طول بلا عرض ولعله أراد بالطويل الطول لأنه متصف بالطول بمعنی دارز شدن وإن كان عينه بمعنى درازي وهذا المتمحل وإن صح إطلاق الطويل عليه لكن لا يصحح قوله وبهذا المعنى الخ لأنه بهذا المعنى قيل : كل خط طول ولا يجري في قوله : كل سطح عريض وكل جسم عميق .
قوله : ( وفي المباحث المشرقية الخ) تأييد لما ذكره سابقاً من أن في الأطول بالقياس إلى الطويل إضافتين وبالقياس إلى غير الأطول ثلاث إضافات .
(
قوله : ( كما أن لمجموعها أيضاً إضافة إليه تحقق الأول والثاني، وإن كان لا يستلزم تحقق الثالث ولا تعقله إلا أن الكلام في الأبعاد الجسمية فلذا كان للأولين إضافة إلى الثالث فإن تعين الطويل والعرض باعتبار العمق .
قوله : ( ولو عبر عنها بالإضافة الثانية لكان أظهر فإن قلت الأطول الذي ذكره المصنف من الطول بمعني أطول الامتدادين، ففيه زيادتان وطول هو إضافة أيضاً، فيصح القول بان الأطول اعتبر فيه إضافة ثالثة بلا تكلف قلت الطول المذكور بمعنى الامتداد مطلقاً وليس إضافة، والأقرب في توجيه كلام المصنف أن يقال : الأطول من زاد طوله على طول غيره بالنسبة إلى قصير ففيه الطولان الإضافيان والزيادة الإضافية فلا غبار في الكلام.
Second observation - Third purpose: The three dimensions of the body. It is said for thickness: it is the filling between surfaces, meaning the mathematical body which is enclosed by one surface or two surfaces or surfaces without additional condition, and in this sense it is said: if a body, then it is in itself deep. And it is said for descending thickness, i.e., thickness qualified by descent, and it is then called ascending thickness, i.e., qualified by ascent, thickness, and in this regard it is said: depth of the well and thickness of the minaret. And it is said: length and width and depth have other meanings besides what was mentioned, such as it is said: length is the extension extending from the center of the world to its circumference, which is the fourth of the meanings of length. And it is said: length also is the extension extending from a human's head to his feet and from the head of quadrupeds to their rear, and this is its meaning. And it is said: width is the extension from a human's right or quadrupeds' right to their left, which is the fourth of the meanings of width. And it is said: depth is the extension from a human's chest to his back and from the back of quadrupeds to the ground, and this is the fourth of the meanings of depth. And know that these mentioned meanings of length, width, and depth are some pure quantities, like length in the sense of the single extension which is the line, width in the sense of the surface, and depth in the sense of thickness. His saying: 'It is the filling between surfaces' thus in a place in the Shifa and in another place it is said depth for the thickness that the surfaces enclose, the intended meaning of 'filling' is that with which it is filled, and the addition of 'filling' to 'what' is lamiyya as is apparent, i.e., filling of an imagined void between surfaces. And it is said that the word 'what' is indefinite and relative, and the addition of 'filling' to it is explicative, thus directing 'filling'. His saying: 'which is enclosed by one surface' like the solid sphere, he indicated by this that mentioning surface as an example, since the intention is not to give a comprehensive and exclusive definition but only a general application to the meanings. His saying: 'and it is said length etc.' he did not combine them with the previous meanings, indicating the abundance of the first applications. His saying: 'from the center of the world to its circumference' like the length of a human, which is the dimension in which the first movement of growth occurs, thus in the Shifa. His saying: 'to the ground' i.e., to its lower part which is on the side of the ground. His saying: 'the single extension which is the line' he states here that the first meaning is the line itself, and it indicates. His saying: 'filling between surfaces meaning the mathematical body etc.' The expression of the text, since it apparently does not include the mathematical body that is in the solid sphere, for it has one surface not multiple surfaces, and includes the natural body, the commentator interpreted it explicitly as intended, although his taking it from the author's phrase is strained. And if he had replaced 'meaning' with 'i.e.', it would be clearer. Then what is in his saying 'filling between surfaces' is either an intensified indefinite or a relative, and the addition of 'filling' to it is explicative. In both cases the intended meaning of 'filling' is not the verbal noun nor the product as thought, but rather the filled thing either terminologically or as a linguistic metaphor. [PAGE_068] Second observation - Third purpose: The three dimensions of the body. C which is the mathematical body. And among them are quantities taken with addition to another matter, like the second assumed or first or third: for the extension being assumed second is an addition to the first assumed and vice versa, and being assumed third is an addition to the sum of the first two; just as the sum also has an addition to it. And sometimes a third addition is considered with it (i.e., with the quantity), like 'longer' (al-atwal): for it is longer in relation to what is long measured against a short one, so here there are two additions: the longer-ness and the length that is relative to shortness. But he expressed the longer-ness as the third addition because it is accidental to a third matter after two matters between which length is realized, or because he considered that shortness is also an addition opposite to length. But there is distance because shortness is not taken with the longer. And if he had expressed it as the second addition, it would be clearer. Or a fourth addition, like 'longer' in relation to another 'longer' in relation to yet another, so there are three additions: two longer-nesses and one relative length. The first longer-ness is accidental to a fourth matter, so he made it a fourth addition by analogy to what preceded, but making it third is preferable. And in the Eastern Investigations, these quantities when taken as added to something, they are sometimes taken such that it is not a condition of their addition to that thing that they be added to something else. And sometimes they are taken such that it is a condition of their addition to something that they be added to a third thing. Example of the first: it is said this line is long when it is said: upon it his saying: i.e., in itself dimension and single extension. So the apparent is that he should say: every line is in itself length, as occurred in the Tajrid: the line is length without width. Perhaps he intended by 'long' (tawil) length (tul), because it is qualified by length in the sense of 'having length' even if it is the same as 'length' in the sense of 'extension'. And this strained interpretation, even if it validates applying 'long' to it, does not validate his saying 'and in this sense etc.' because in this sense it is said: every line is length, and it does not apply in his saying: every surface is wide and every body is deep. His saying: 'and in the Eastern Investigations etc.' supports what he mentioned previously: that in 'longer' in relation to 'long' there are two additions, and in relation to other than 'longer' there are three additions. His saying: 'just as for their sum also there is an addition to it' realizes the first and second, although it does not necessitate realizing the third nor conceiving it, except that the discourse is about bodily dimensions, so for the first two there is an addition to the third, for length and width are determined by depth. His saying: 'and if he had expressed it as the second addition it would be clearer' then if you say: the 'longer' that the author mentioned is from length in the sense of the longer of two extensions, so it has two increases and length is also an addition, so it is correct to say that in 'longer' a third addition is considered without strain. I say: the mentioned length is in the sense of extension absolutely and is not an addition. The closest in directing the author's words is to say: 'longer' is he whose length increased over another's length in relation to a short one, so in it there are two relative lengths and a relative increase, so there is no flaw in the speech.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the three dimensions (length, width, depth) in multiple senses, followed by commentary. The text first defines thickness (thikhn) as the filling between surfaces, i.e., the mathematical body enclosed by one or more surfaces. It notes that in this sense, any body is deep in itself. It then distinguishes between descending thickness (depth of a well) and ascending thickness (thickness of a minaret). Next, it lists four meanings for each dimension: length as (1) the single extension (line), (2) from center of world to circumferences, (3) from head to foot of humans or animals, (4) the extension from right to left? Actually the fourth meaning of length is from center of world to circumference. The text then clarifies that some of these meanings are pure quantities (e.g., length as line, width as surface, depth as thickness) while others involve addition (e.g., specific orientations). The commentary on 'filling between surfaces' explains that 'filling' (hashw) refers to what fills, and the phrase is interpreted as either indefinite or relative, with an explicative addition. It notes that the example of a single surface (solid sphere) is illustrative, not a strict definition. The commentary also discusses the mathematical vs. natural body and the strained interpretation of the author's phrase. On page 68, the commentary continues with how these quantities are taken with addition, e.g., the 'longer' (atwal) involves two or three additions depending on relativity. It cites the Eastern Investigations on conditionality of addition. The discussion ends with clarification that 'longer' means one whose length exceeds another relative to a short one, involving two relative lengths and a relative increase.

Key Terms
طول (tul - length)
عرض (ard - width)
عمق (umq - depth)
امتداد (imtidad - extension)
إضافة (idafa - addition/relation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which dimension has a meaning involving 'from the center of the world to its circumference'?
A) Width
B) Depth
C) Length
D) Thickness

Key Takeaway
Length, width, and depth each have multiple meanings, including astronomical and anatomical senses, beyond their pure quantitative definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If a single physical dimension like length can have multiple valid meanings (line, astronomical, anatomical), how does that challenge your assumption that theological terms have only one straightforward definition? What might you need to re-examine?
?
The passage distinguishes between 'pure quantities' and relational extensions. Think of a concept in your own life (e.g., 'success', 'love', 'justice') that you have treated as a pure quantity. How would your understanding change if you considered its relational meanings as equally valid?
?
When you encounter a difficult term in Islamic texts, do you tend to settle for one meaning or actively explore multiple dimensions? What is one term you could study more deeply this week, inspired by this lesson's approach?
